Yep, Big Z. The Cleveland Cavaliers\u2019 affable big man earned two spots (2003, 2005). Still, given Sabonis\u2019 background, legacy and genetics, it would be reasonable to think that the Indiana Pacers big man had been preceded by, say, his own father, Arvydas, in participating in one of the league\u2019s highest honors. Someone teased that he now had basketball bragging rights over his dad, a Hall of Famer, in at least one category. The younger Sabonis wouldn\u2019t go there. \u201cI\u2019m never saying anything like that. For me, he\u2019s one of the greatest,\u201d he said. \u201cDifferent eras, different players. It\u2019s completely different.\u201d Arvydas Sabonis arguably is one of the greatest European basketball players of all time and one of the NBA\u2019s biggest \u201cwhat if?\u201d stories. When he entered the league at 30 with Portland in 1995-96 , the 7-foot-3, 250-pound center had morphed through injuries and time into a different performer altogether. In his NBA debut on Nov. 3, 1995 , Sabonis scored 10 points with seven rebounds and flashed some of the finesse that stole fans\u2019 breath . But he moved gingerly and more slowly, toting an extra 40 or 50 pounds \u2014 not out of shape but beefy and land-bound, an older version of his former self. Arvydas Sabonis became the stuff of YouTube legend , separated from his son Domantas\u2019 career not just by a generation but by a dramatic shift in global and sports politics. The father was the better player. The son is getting opportunities his old man never had. \u201cI talked to my father last night for a while,\u201d the newly crowned All-Star told NBA.com two weeks ago, the morning after the reserves were announced. \u201cEverybody\u2019s enjoying it. It\u2019s really amazing for our family and I.\u201d Arvydas was fairly inscrutable in his seven seasons with the Blazers, equally quick to smile or scowl but never an effusive interview. Did he ever get wistful about the years he lost behind Iron Curtain, his skills essentially held hostage by the Soviet Union\u2019s dictates that he deploy his skills for their glory only? Few ever knew, because he didn\u2019t share. But there\u2019s no mistaking the love he has for the game and for the family\u2019s most accomplished torch bearer. Brothers Zygimantas, 28 (\u201cZiggy\u201d) and Tautvydas, 27 (\u201cTuti\u201d) both stand 6-foot-9 and competed in Europe. But the youngest of the three, \u201cDomas,\u201d took the family business to a new, higher level: first as an import at Gonzaga University and then at age 20 as the 11th pick in the 2016 Draft by Orlando. \u201cHe watches every game. He always points out a couple things he sees,\u201d Sabonis told NBA.com of his father\u2019s interest and pride. The entire family, including his mother Ingrida (the first \u201cMiss Lithuania\u201d in 1988, by the way) and sister, Ausrine, is expected to be on hand for All-Star weekend. Domantas had the hoops mythology on his side \u2014 he was born between Games 4 and 5 of Portland\u2019s 1996 first-round series against Utah. After his dad\u2019s NBA retirement in 2003 , the family moved to beachside Malaga, Spain (which explains Domantas\u2019 proficiency in three languages). He played for a junior team in that city as a 15-year-old and at 17 was vying with grown men in the Euroleague . But as far as actual, Sabonis-family development, Domantas said: \u201cWe grew up in the basketball world. My dad never encouraged us to play. We just grew up going to his games in Portland. We had basketballs in our hands and just decided we were going to play. \u201cWe never played 1-on-1 but we played shooting games. Pickup games with his friends. Half court.\u201d Their NBA styles are different, owing somewhat to age. His father was massive, surpassed in size and strength only by Shaquille O\u2019Neal. He had a reliable mid-range shot, a sweeping hook and uncanny court vision. Domantas is four inches shorter, leaner and lefthanded. But he shares some of that vision and likes to find teammates with those slick one-handed passes. He especially is a weapon in the Pacers\u2019 pick-and-roll, previously (and again lately) with Victor Oladipo, now with Malcolm Brogdon too. \u201cYou can tell in many ways he\u2019s probably the heart and soul of this group,\u201d Miami coach Erik Spoelstra told The Athletic last month. \u201cThey play a physical brand of basketball and he\u2019s arguably one of the most physical players in the league. But he does it with intelligence \u2014 he\u2019s not just plowing over people \u2014 he has a skill set that he brings and he\u2019s big.\u201d Domantas Sabonis\u2019 \u201cfeel\u201d for the game strikes teammates and opponents alike as the basketball characteristic he most inherited from Arvydas. \u201cFeel? For sure,\u201d he said. \u201cI personally don\u2019t see comparisons. But my family and my dad\u2019s friends, they grew up watching my dad play, they say they see so many similarities it\u2019s crazy.\u201d A player\u2019s first All-Star selection often comes in a breakthrough season. In Sabonis\u2019 case, though, it\u2019s an honor that has more to do with his increased role than any dramatic step up in performance. To date, he\u2019s averaging 18.3 ppg, 12.5 rpg and 4.8 apg \u2013 all career highs. This comes after back-to-back seasons of steady improvement (11.6 ppg, 7.7 rpg in 2017-18 and 14.1 ppg, 9.3 rpg last season). The big difference in 2019-20 is that Sabonis has started 52 out of 52 games, teaming up with fellow big Myles Turner. Last season, he came off the bench for all but five games, finishing second in Kia Sixth Man Award balloting. So Sabonis is doing what he\u2019s done. He\u2019s just doing it now for three-quarters of each game rather than half. \u201cAs far as what he\u2019s done this season, he was doing it last year,\u201d Pacers coach Nate McMillan said. \u201cHe rebounded for us, he initiated the offense, we played through him on the offensive end. At times post-ups or the pick-and-roll action. The fact that he\u2019s in the starting lineup, he\u2019s getting more minutes.\u201d With Oladipo out a year while recovering from quadricep surgery , Sabonis\u2019 place in the Pacers\u2019 pecking order rose. The conference coaches needed to account for Indiana\u2019s 31-17 record at the time he was selected (they promptly lost six in a row before beating Milwaukee Wednesday ). The reserve spot was seen, too, as a reward for Sabonis\u2019 consistency, work habits and 41 double-doubles (and four triple-doubles). He also kept up with Oladipo (\u201818, \u201819) in their pact to prove the 2017 Paul George trade didn\u2019t favor Oklahoma City after all. \u201cIt\u2019s beyond deserving,\u201d teammate T.J. McConnell said of Sabonis. \u201cWe wouldn\u2019t be where we are without him. He\u2019s carried us.\u201d McConnell said he \u201chated\u201d playing against Sabonis when they were foes. \u201cWhen I was in Philly, he was just one of those dudes who changed the game every time he was in,\u201d McConnell said. \u201cYou can\u2019t really stop him \u2014 he does a bit of everything. He makes the right play. He gets people open. He makes your job easier, so you can\u2019t ask for much more than that.\u201d Said Bulls coach Jim Boylen: \u201cWhat people don\u2019t realize about him is his physicality. He wears you down, he just pounds on you and gets to that left hand. It\u2019s very difficult to handle him.\u201d There\u2019s something poetic in Sabonis\u2019 terrific 2019-20 season hinging on the newfound opportunities he has had in Indiana\u2019s system, because of the opportunities denied to his father three decades ago. Arvydas might as well have wanted to play on Mars \u2014 he wasn\u2019t coming to the NBA in those pre-Glasnost and Perestroika days. In March 1990, Lithuania became the first republic to break away from the Soviet Union. Then it took several more years for his body to feel right enough to test it against O\u2019Neal, Tim Duncan, David Robinson, Alonzo Mourning and the rest.  Had the father arrived in the NBA at his son\u2019s age, league history \u2014 certainly, Blazers history and family history \u2014 might have been different. Former Blazers coach Mike Dunleavy once told The Oregonian : \u201cThere\u2019s no question that before he came over, he was one of the top three centers in the world, right there with [Kareem] Abdul-Jabbar and [Bill] Walton. He could run like a deer, shoot, pass. He would have been incredible.\u201d Missed but not forgotten, that pretty much sums up Arvydas Sabonis\u2019 prime. Even the creakier version etched himself into memories, though. During Wednesday\u2019s Lakers-Nuggets game, announcers Dave Pasch, Jimmy Dykes and Jeff Van Gundy marveled over Denver center Nikola Jokic\u2019s passing skill and searched for comparisons. \u201cWalton,\u201d one said. And then: \u201cSabonis.\u201d Van Gundy quickly clarified: \u201c Older Sabonis. And his son\u2019s a great passer too, but not like Arvydas.\u201d Domantas, though, is keeping the family business running. And business, particularly this weekend, is good. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. You can e-mail him here , find his archive here and follow him on Twitter . The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":28704,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/luka-doncic-trade-young-separate-paths-stardom","date":"2020-02-14","title":"Time to stop the second-guessing","content":" There are two baseball caps \u2014 one bearing the logo of the Atlanta Hawks, the other of the Dallas Mavericks \u2014 that are valuable yet unaccounted for today. They were last seen at the 2018 NBA Draft on the heads of Luka Doncic and Trae Young, where they stayed just long enough to collect sweaty DNA. In a sense, they just didn\u2019t fit. Moments before NBA commissioner Adam Silver issued those obligatory caps to the freshly drafted players onstage, the Hawks and Mavericks agreed to a stunning swap, Doncic for Young. It was a jolt not only for the teams involved but the players themselves, and in the haze that followed, those keepsakes vanished. Young isn\u2019t sure if he still has the Mavericks cap. Doncic shrugs when asked; maybe his Hawks cap is in Dallas or perhaps stashed back in his homeland of Slovenia. This much they do know: Doncic and Young didn\u2019t exchange them backstage; only pleasantries. \u201cGood luck,\u201d Young told Doncic. \u201cSee you soon,\u201d Doncic told Young. Two years later, Doncic and Young are still linked, and based on the circumstances and results so far, they\u2019ll probably be twin basketball brothers from other mothers until their careers are done. That\u2019s because long after the second-guessing over the Draft-night trade began to die down, Doncic and Young are leaving marks like few second-year players have in league history. As for Luka: He came to the States a bit starstruck at age 19 and famously begged for the jersey of LeBron James the first time they met on the court. And then, seeing Doncic stockpile historic triple-doubles and become a global box office sensation, LeBron became a big fan and wanted his jersey. As for Trae: The Hawks aren\u2019t ready to put a statue of Young next to the one honoring Dominique Wilkins outside State Farm Arena just yet. But if he keeps dropping 40 points and fooling defenders with nutmeg dribbles , Atlanta might do him one better and rename one of its main avenues Peachtrae Street . Both average 29 points and nine assists and rank in the top five in those categories while playing at an efficient level especially given their ball dominance. Doncic also averages nine rebounds. They\u2019re the youngest All-Star starters in 15 years. \u201cIt\u2019s a unique situation, me and him,\u201d Young said. \u201cHe\u2019s been playing at a crazy level. I think I\u2019ve been playing pretty well. At the same time we\u2019re both running on different paths and roads. We hoop and compete and battle every night. We both want to end up being great.\u201d * * * Everywhere he goes, he\u2019s a head-turning sensation, stealing the applause from bigger NBA stars, even the ones he YouTubed as a boy not long ago. The rise of Doncic in NBA fandom has been swift and somewhat surprising if only because, right up until the Draft, he was a ghost to most. Such is the case with European-born players. They can be sensational in Spain, France and Greece. Doncic dominated when he won back-to-back Rising Star awards, the season MVP and Final Four MVP in the EuroLeague as a teenager . The problem: Those players didn\u2019t play at Kentucky, or beat Duke, or get dubbed a five-star recruit, or destroy anyone\u2019s bracket in the NCAA tournament. But now? Doncic trailed only LeBron in the fan voting for the West All-Star team. Kids everywhere can spell Slovenia and find it on a map. Opposing players are raving about him and seeking him after games to give props. \u201cThat young man,\u201d said Clippers defensive ace Pat Beverley, \u201cis a major problem.\u201d Doncic is laying the groundwork for legendary status with almost every game and week. He doesn\u2019t turn 21 until Feb. 28, and after just 16 months in the league he has a slew of youngest-ever triple-double accomplishments, all while being the face of a franchise and maybe someday, the face of the league. He\u2019s the rare talent who pleases the purists and connects with the current generation. He may not be a one-in-a-million talent but those are the odds of finding someone who isn\u2019t a Doncic fan. He\u2019s comfortable anywhere on the floor, making him more dangerous, flexible and effective. His jumper, in terms of stroke and splash, is already mature in its purity. He has mastered the Harden-esque step-back move to instantly reposition himself from a long 2-pointer to a 3-pointer and create daylight between the man guarding him. He can dribble in traffic between his legs and around his back, with the left or right hand, and brings a gentle ambidextrous touch at the rim. And he\u2019s always moving toward making a play; there\u2019s very little wasted motion. He is 20 going on 30 in basketball wisdom and that\u2019s why Warriors coach Steve Kerr was moved to compare him to Larry Bird and add: \u201cHe\u2019s a step ahead in a chess match. He\u2019s different. He\u2019s got this incredible knack for seeing the floor and knowing what\u2019s coming next. He plays with a smile on his face and is obviously having fun. Gonna be a cornerstone in this league for a long time.\u201d  The nuances of the game don\u2019t befuddle him. He\u2019s a quick study in terms of flow, figuring out defenses and angles, understanding whom to pass to and when, and how to use his 6-foot-8 height for mismatches. \u201cGetting in the paint is one of my keys,\u201d Doncic has said. \u201cEvery time I get in the paint a lot we score a lot. We work on it. I\u2019ve got so many ways to improve. Got to work harder.\u201d Obviously, Doncic had a head start as a professional, becoming seasoned by playing with Real Madrid starting at 16. He won Kia Rookie of the Year but it was an unfair contest, really, over Young and everyone else, given his European experience. He came to the NBA and the United States better equipped to deal with the geographical change than Dirk Nowitzki, who grappled somewhat with the culture, language and game as a Dallas rookie. \u201cDirk wasn\u2019t as mature or worldly as Luka,\u201d Mavericks owner Mark Cuban said. \u201cDirk came over here adapting and trying to live his life. Luka is street smart. Luka has been through it. Sure, it was a change for Luka and he had to adapt as well, but nothing like what Dirk went through.\u201d Doncic blends in the locker room with players from much meaner streets, already knows the lingo and the American swag. Elsewhere, though, it\u2019s a bit more of a challenge; his English is still in development and he is understandably stoic and careful in interviews, which he prefers to keep to a minimum. He speaks with his head down, rarely making eye contact. This is not being disrespectful. Doncic doesn\u2019t want the attention to overwhelm his teammates. He was sensitive about what his arrival last year did to Dennis Smith Jr., who was initially the young flavor of the moment in Dallas. Smith was traded to the Knicks once it became clear the ball was best in Doncic\u2019s hands. The Mavericks adding Doncic so soon after Dirk\u2019s retirement seems like Steve Young following Joe Montana with the San Francisco 49ers. The transition is just so convenient and smooth, although the Mavs tend to pump the brakes on perception even while pinching themselves. \u201cI stay away from the comparison game,\u201d Mavs coach Rick Carlisle said. \u201cI think it\u2019s important that this kid is his own player, and he truly is. Look, each month he continues to develop and learn more and hones certain skills even more. It\u2019s all pretty exciting to watch, so yes, I understand the hype.\u201d Doncic averaged a triple double for December and went 42-11-12 against the Spurs when Carlisle gushed \u201che can do anything he wants on the basketball court,\u201d and that stoked conversation about him on the path to being a generational player. \u201cCrazy, you know?\u201d Doncic said. \u201cI appreciate all the respect from players, all the fans voting for me for All-Star. It\u2019s something when I was young, I never imagined. Being voted like that, hearing from the greats. Really glad, really happy, thanks to all.\u201d * * *  The 2017-18 season was one for the junkyard for the Hawks and Mavericks who won 24 games apiece and stared at long rehabs. They were used as load management games, mostly ignored by the major networks when filling out the national TV schedule, invisible and off radar. In those situations, it\u2019s not unusual for focus to shift quickly toward the future. At one point while losing 14 of their final 16 games, Carlisle quizzed Mavericks personnel chief Donnie Nelson about the upcoming Draft: \u201cWho\u2019s the best player coming out?\u201d \u201cIt\u2019s Luka Doncic and it\u2019s not close.\u201d Carlisle said recently: \u201cWe got into a deeper conversation and Donnie says he\u2019s got the elements of some of the great international players of all time. He said Luka was very much an ori ginal. \u201d Considering that Nowitzki was winding down his career in Dallas and as perhaps the best international player ever, the Mavs were all-in on Doncic. Problem: They held the fifth pick; Doncic would surely be gone by then. Solution: They were desperate, which means they would aggressively seek to move up. In Atlanta, the Hawks were in the hands of Travis Schlenk, starting his second season as the architect after leaving the Warriors. Schlenk saw point-blank how the Warriors used wise drafting (Stephen Curry, Klay Thompson and Draymond Green) to build a Goliath with staying power and wanted the same model in Atlanta. The previous year in his first Draft in Atlanta, Schlenk took forward John Collins at 19 with success and was open to adding additional picks. His philosophy: Better to have as many swings at the plate as possible to increase your chances of hitting a homer. The Hawks had the third pick in 2018 and were high on Doncic and almost equally so on Young. They wanted someone with talent but also the ability to entertain. The Hawks were a soft sell in Atlanta and chairman Tony Ressler wanted fans in the seats at newly renovated State Farm Arena. Both the Hawks and Mavericks were convinced the Suns, who already had Devin Booker, would go big with the No. 1 pick; Deandre Ayton played down the road at Arizona. The Kings had the second pick and GM Vlade Divac was once an international player himself so there was concern. But guards De\u2019Aaron Fox and Buddy Hield meshed in their first season together, so the Kings also went big with Marvin Bagley III. Also, the Grizzlies at four were sold on Jaren Jackson Jr. When Nelson called Schlenk and upped their offer to a top-five protected pick in 2019 just to swap No. 5 for No. 3, it sparked intense conversation within the Hawks.  \u201cThe Mavericks made the offer and I took it to the group,\u201d Schlenk said. \u201cWe had 16 guys in there and the vote was 8-8. What it came down to is we projected Dallas to finish low the following year and we\u2019d get another lottery pick, and lottery picks were our most valuable assets at the time.\u201d Cuban said the wait to hear back from the Hawks felt like an eternity in Dallas. \u201cAnd then the anxiety increases after you make the trade,\u201d he said. \u201cTrying to get it done is one level of anxiety, but the anxiety after you get the guy? Because look how many picks that don\u2019t pan out, who are not stars. You could see that Luka had something. We knew the kid could be special. But some guys are just rookie hype, too. Until you know, you just don\u2019t know. I just want the guy who takes us to the next level.\u201d The public reaction was favorable in Dallas, mixed in Atlanta \u2014 surprising, considering most fans and media had seen Young but not Doncic. But Young finished the last few weeks poorly in his final season at Oklahoma. And then he looked dreadful in NBA Summer League at Salt Lake City. \u201cTrae had nothing to do with the trade,\u201d Schlenk said. \u201cHe didn\u2019t ask for it. Because of that there was a ton of pressure on him. People were looking for reasons for why Trae was going to fail.\u201d The extra No. 1 pick was used in the 2019 Draft on swingman Cam Reddish, who\u2019s having a somewhat rough start in Atlanta. Ultimately his development will weigh in the final verdict. \u201cPeople will say what they want but from what I see, both teams made out fine,\u201d Carlisle said. * * * He is slightly built but not too fragile to carry a grudge. That\u2019s how Young approached his NBA career, by using 30-foot jumpers and no-look passes to attack anyone who doubted him. No, the world has nothing against Young; actually, the world has more important issues to deal with. And although his All-Star berth caused a stir because the Hawks aren\u2019t winning, he did win the fan vote which disputed any notion of unpopularity. Still, for an athlete in the social media age, seeing a dozen critical tweets seems like a million. And a fair amount of comments directed toward Young made sure to mention how well Doncic was doing and where the Mavericks stood in the standings. When Young dropped 42 points with eight 3-pointers in a win over the Nuggets back in November, he went to Twitter and announced in all caps: YOUR APOLOGY NEEDS TO BE AS LOUD AS THE DISRESPECT WAS. It has received more than 150,000 likes, including one from Ravens quarterback Lamar Jackson, who could relate. Schlenk said: \u201cIt\u2019s just society today. He\u2019s got the toughest skin I\u2019ve been around. But with social media, anyone in the world can say something to you, tell you what they think. Maybe on some level it bothers him but he copes with it unbelievably well. It\u2019s motivation. Look, he wants to be great and he\u2019ll get there because he\u2019s a smart player with a high IQ.\u201d The offensive numbers being posted by Young are epic; good luck finding a dozen players in history who ranked top five in scoring and assists in a single season. He\u2019s a shot-making and passing wizard. Young\u2019s defense is another issue although most young players struggle at first, and until he gets physically stronger, Young\u2019s size doesn\u2019t help. Anyway, Young has the support of the one person in Atlanta who carries the most respected voice, someone also depicted as one-dimensional, yet became a Hall of Famer. \u201cIt\u2019s wonderful for us to have a guy like him,\u201d Hawks legend Dominique Wilkins said. \u201cHe\u2019s got a lot of heart and that\u2019s putting it lightly. We knew his ability and his talent. Once I saw him play in a couple of games, I said he\u2019s got the \u2018it-factor.\u2019 Players know players. He wasn\u2019t hard to figure out. You knew what he would become.\u201d And there\u2019s a familiar voice outside of Atlanta who brings the same clout. \u201cHe deserves being the All-Star starter in the Eastern Conference,\u201d Doncic said. \u201cHe\u2019s playing like a beast this year.\u201d Young and Doncic are in different situations. The Mavericks are built to win 45-50 games and compete for the playoffs. The Hawks are under construction and loaded with pups. Plus, Collins missed 25 games for a violation of the NBA\u2019s Anti-Drug Program . Therefore, it\u2019s not totally fair to conclude that Young isn\u2019t a winner. He\u2019s sensitive to that perception, worried it could swell into a reputation. \u201cEven when I led the country in points and assists at Oklahoma I\u2019d get frustrated when we\u2019d lose,\u201d Young said. \u201cI had 49 once and couldn\u2019t enjoy it because we lost. Those loses stick with me. You can\u2019t enjoy it when you\u2019re a competitor like me.\u201d Young\u2019s skills are refined and surprisingly disciplined for someone who makes high-risk choices. His shooting range is deep; those 30-footers began in middle school when everyone began to grow except Young and it was the only way he could launch without being blocked. He challenges bigger players at the rim. He can shoot the mid-range, rare nowadays for players with an appetite for 3-pointers. But it\u2019s his court vision and passing that also separate him from other big scorers. \u201cI said at the time we drafted him, that\u2019s what stood out,\u201d Schlenk said. \u201cHe got all the hype for shooting threes. And he\u2019s got that skill set. But his ability to make passes, with his right and left hand, off the dribble and off the pick and roll, is what makes him unique.\u201d That along with his crafty dribble, which he\u2019ll often use for his signature move, the nutmeg \u2014 a pass or dribble between the defender\u2019s legs. When that happens there\u2019s only one thing to say to the victim: \u201c You got Trae\u2019d .\u201d \u201cI don\u2019t have to be entertaining but I choose to,\u201d Young said. \u201cSince I was a kid I\u2019ve always played with a lot of swag, a lot of flair. That\u2019s something that\u2019s always been in my game. I\u2019d practice those things as a kid. I\u2019d go into the gym with my dad and we didn\u2019t even shoot the whole day. We\u2019d practice behind-the-back passes, left hand, right hand, stand across the court from each other, I\u2019d work on one-dribble bounce passes and lobs. \u201cI\u2019ve had a couple of players this year come over and tell me, `Man, you a bad MF.\u2019 It\u2019s respect. It\u2019s pretty cool that guys will say that to me during or after the game.\u201d There\u2019s a good chance before or after Saturday\u2019s game in Dallas that Young will be approached by a fellow All-Star starter if only because they seem magnetized to each other. \u201cMe and Luka are cool and we talk now and then,\u201d Young said. \u201cWe\u2019re going to be compared until the end of our careers. It\u2019s not going anywhere.\u201d * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. You can e-mail him here , find his archive here  and follow him on Twitter  . The NBA announced a number of changes to this year\u2019s NBA All-Star Game, which airs live at 8 p.m. ET, including a new fourth quarter format that will honor the late Kobe Bryant, who earned 18 NBA All-Star selections and a record-tying four NBA All-Star Game MVP awards. Additional special elements during TNT\u2019s NBA All-Star Game presentation will include a Los Angeles Lakers-themed tribute to Bryant within the virtual shot clock \u2013 displaying a combination of gold and purple accents at 24 seconds and eight seconds remaining on the clock during the fourth quarter, representing Bryant\u2019s jersey numbers. \u201cPresenting the fourth quarter completely free of any commercial breaks creates a unique opportunity to showcase the new All-Star Game format, provide the best fan experience, and align with the league\u2019s overarching plans to pay tribute to Kobe Bryant this weekend,\u201d said Craig Barry, EVP and Chief Content Officer, Turner Sports. On Sunday, Team Giannis and Team LeBron will compete to win each of the first three quarters, all of which will start with the score of 0-0 and will be 12 minutes long. At the beginning of the fourth quarter, the game clock will be turned off and a Final Target Score will be set. The Final Target Score will be determined by taking the leading team\u2019s total cumulative score through three quarters and adding 24 points \u2013 the 24 representing Bryant\u2019s jersey number for the final 10 seasons of his NBA career. The teams will then play an untimed fourth quarter and the first team to reach the Final Target Score will win the NBA All-Star Game. Sunday\u2019s coverage will begin with the TNT NBA Tip-Off presented by Autotrader pregame show at 6 p.m. ET, featuring the Sports Emmy Award-winning studio team of Ernie Johnson, Charles Barkley, Kenny Smith and Shaquille O\u2019Neal. Derrick Rose lived that fantasy. The fantasy every youngster in this fierce, basketball-mad city dared to dream at one time or another, to represent Chicago in a Bulls uniform and do it at the highest level. The youngest Kia MVP in league history , before Stephen Curry and James Harden, Kevin Durant and Kawhi Leonard, Giannis Antetokounmpo and Luka Doncic, there was young Derrick Rose. \u201cHe\u2019s a living, flesh-and-blood embodiment of all our dreams,\u201d Hall of Famer and Chicago hoops icon Isiah Thomas said of Rose. \u201cBecause every kid in the city grew up dreaming of playing for the Chicago Bulls and hearing our name called running out of that tunnel. That\u2019s every kid\u2019s dream. And Derrick Rose is the one \u2026 basically, he was the chosen one to live that dream. \u201cAnd not only did he live it, he became MVP of the league,\u201d Thomas said. \u201cIt doesn\u2019t get any higher than that. It just doesn\u2019t. That\u2019s why you not only have to see him and protect him, you have to make sure he gets the right knowledge around him, because you know he could be the voice out of Chicago that speaks to the world.\u201d A basketball prodigy born and raised in the city, Rose will receive a hero\u2019s welcome during All-Star 2020 in his hometown. But it\u2019s not the storybook ending Chicagoans wished it would be, with Rose representing the Bulls during the league\u2019s showcase weekend. He\u2019s not returning as an All-Star, though he\u2019s certainly performed well enough this season in Detroit to have warranted serious consideration. He originally was tapped to be on center stage All-Star Saturday night as a competitor in the Taco Bell Skills Competition he won as a rookie in 2009. Instead, he had to withdraw because of an adductor strain. Still, he will feel that Chicago love. The kind Thomas said you only get back home, when it feels like an entire city can shelter its own from the cold, harsh realities they might face outside of that cocoon. But even in the weeks leading up to the All-Star announcements, when there was a buzz and still a chance he might complete a comeback story for the ages and be named an All-Star, Rose didn\u2019t get lost in the moment. \u201cNah, I never did it for that, I never in my life played to be in that game,\u201d Rose said. \u201cIf people decide to put me in there, that\u2019s cool. But if not, I\u2019m going to keep playing the way that I\u2019m playing. \u201d Chicago\u2019s rich basketball roots run deep. It\u2019s a generational love affair with the game, a passion that has to be experienced to wrap your head around why it means so much to so many. It goes beyond the city limits, too, spreading in every direction throughout the \u201cChicagoland\u201d area, which has produced some of the greatest and most impactful players in NBA history . From Joliet\u2019s George Mikan, the league\u2019s first superstar in the 1940s and 50s; to Thomas, a West Sider who is generally regarded as the best player ever from the city; all the way to Dwyane Wade, a surefire future Hall of Famer; and now Los Angeles Lakers superstar Anthony Davis, who carries the torch for Chicago as one of the league\u2019s five best current players. The list is long and legendary, filled with prep and college stars who reached various levels of fame and achievement in the NBA. But Rose is the only one to have done it in a Bulls uniform and the only one to earn the distinction of Kia Most Valuable Player, when he captured the honor in 2011 at just 22 years old. \u201cThere\u2019s a historical love that is always acknowledged and appreciated by players who come out of Chicago because of what we saw our mothers, fathers and grandmothers have to go through,\u201d Thomas said. \u201cTheir struggle, their everyday struggle, was in our face. In our house. No food, no heat, no lights. But we always seen them maintain and keep their dignity. \u201cAnd that goes into our sport, in the way you compete, the way you never give up, the way you accept all the challenges and the way you don\u2019t cry about it,\u201d Thomas said. \u201cSo when you come to a basketball game and it\u2019s time to compete and you rise up to the highest level, in our sport, you\u2019re a mythical figure. And for us, what Derrick Rose represents and what he still represents is that you can overcome the struggle. Not only can you overcome it, even when you get knocked down, you can still get up and walk. That\u2019s what Derrick Rose is for all of us.\u201d At his zenith, Rose captivated fans and his NBA peers from the city, past, present and future. And that still resonates today. \u201cHe was like a once in a lifetime athlete, even to this day,\u201d said Hawks forward Jabari Parker, who like Rose, starred and won state championships at Chicago\u2019s famed Simeon Career Academy. \u201cBut for me growing up, it was like he was doing things other NBA guys couldn\u2019t do. His athleticism was at its peak when he was in high school to be honest with you. Obviously in the NBA [before the ACL surgery] he was super athletic. But I remember going to Simeon and watching him practice and he was doing 360 dunks between the legs. I saw it with my own eyes. That\u2019s crazy, at 6-3, to be able to that. He was just on another level.\u201d Parker, the No. 2 pick in the 2014 Draft, said he went to Simeon instead of his neighborhood high school because of Rose, who is seven years older. His father Robert \u201cSonny\u201d Parker, a Chicago legend himself at Farragut Career Academy and the 17th pick in the 1976 NBA Draft, had always schooled his son on the city\u2019s brilliant basketball history. Like Jabari Parker would later, Rose wore No. 25 at Simeon in honor of Ben \u201cBenji\u201d Wilson, the budding star who led Simeon to the 1984 state title before he was murdered by a gang member in his senior year. But Rose was a link to a glorious past, an outstanding present and promising future. \u201cWhen we\u2019re coming up in Chicago, that\u2019s our culture, basketball,\u201d Jabari Parker said. \u201cSimeon was definitely a powerhouse program in the city. But for a while the Red West [division] was the place to be, with Westinghouse, Crane and Marshall. The Red South wasn\u2019t really a thing until Derrick resurrected the Red South and the program at Simeon, because up to that point we hadn\u2019t been a [state championship] power since the late 80s and 90s. He was definitely the reason I went there and we were able to preserve the rich legacy of the program.\u201d  Rose won a city and state title at Simeon as a junior, when he hurried the game-winner in overtime to deliver Simeon\u2019s first championship since that Wilson-led team won it all in 1984. They repeated as the city and state champions his senior year, becoming the first Chicago Public League school to win back-to-back titles. \u201cEverybody remembers that,\u201d said Heat rookie Kendrick Nunn, who won four straight state titles alongside Parker at Simeon, and along with Wilson, Bobby Simmons, Rose and Parker are the only players in school history to have their jersey numbers retired. \u201cI remember people talking about how Derrick had put the national spotlight on Simeon, the public league and the city in general. The whole city felt it.\u201d Rose was generating buzz well beyond Chicagoland by then. The basketball world was certainly aware of him, and that included established pros like Quentin Richardson, who was in the midst of a 13-year NBA career after starring on at state championship team at Whitney Young in 1998 and at DePaul for two seasons before being selected by the LA Clippers with the 18th overall pick in the 2000 NBA Draft.  \u201cThe word was out on him in the city when he was in middle school,\u201d Richardson said. \u201cPeople were talking about him even then, that he was going to be the next one from the city. When I saw him when he was in high school. It was D. Rose and Sherron Collins, who was about to go to Kansas. And it was basically, who you want? D-Rose wasn\u2019t there yet but he was on the way and Sherron was a senior and a McDonald\u2019s All-American. Sherron averaged 30 and was gunning that thing. D. Rose was more of an all-around player, made plays for everybody and just had that next-level instinct. Plus he was taller and had that longer, lean athletic body. You could see he was going to be special even then.\u201d Richardson said Rose\u2019s athleticism, even as a high school player, was jaw-dropping. \u201cHe was just crazy explosive,\u201d Richardson said. \u201cHe was so unassuming. He\u2019d come down the court and everything was so fast and sudden, it was crazy. He was playing 100 miles an hour but never changed his expression. He was so humble and unassuming. And still everybody that saw him knew he was the one. They gave him Benji\u2019s number at Simeon. They knew it, he was the one.\u201d Rockets guard Eric Gordon, Indiana\u2019s Mr Basketball and also a top-five recruit in that high school class of 2007, teamed up with Rose the summer before their senior year and torched the competition from coast to coast on the summer circuit. He said he knew then that Rose was destined for even greater things at the next level. \u201cThat was a different time,\u201d Gordon said. \u201cIt wasn\u2019t like AAU ball today. We had like six or seven guys, at most, on our team. That\u2019s it. And we went around the country taking over every court we touched. It was so much fun.\u201d An Indianapolis native, Gordon said he couldn\u2019t imagine what it must have been like for Rose coming home after his All-American freshman season at Memphis, to the pressures of being the No.1 overall pick with the hopes and dreams of a city and the hometown team on your shoulders. \u201cDerrick made the best of it. I mean, he was the MVP,\u201d said Gordon, who remains close with Rose to this day and still works out with him in the offseason. \u201cThe injuries \u2026 that happens. It\u2019s a part of it. You can\u2019t do anything about it. But look at what he\u2019s come back from to play at a high level now. I can\u2019t say I\u2019m surprised because I know how talented he\u2019s always been and how hard he works. He works like crazy and he just loves the game.\u201d  Rose\u2019s work ethic and love for the game is what endeared him to Bulls fans before an ACL injury and knee issues sidetracked him during what should have been the physical prime of his career. It\u2019s what has carried him through his toughest times as a pro, which includes a nasty fallout with the Bulls, who traded him to New York on June 22, 2016. There was also a very vocal segment of the fan base that turned on Rose when he sat out the entire 2012-13 season recovering from his ACL surgery. He was cleared by the Bulls\u2019 medical staff to return to full contact practice in January but never played in a game. And the Bulls advanced to the conference semifinals without him, losing to eventual champion Miami Heat. \u201cIf the people that criticized him knew him, if they really knew him, they\u2019d have known how much it was tearing him up not to be out there,\u201d Richardson said. \u201cThis is a man that has never cheated the game a day in his life, not one. To have it snatched away like that, it had to be the worst thing ever. And that\u2019s also what makes what he\u2019s done since then so sweet. The boy is back to looking the way he did before all of the injuries.\u201d Pistons coach Dwane Casey certainly thinks so. He was adamant that Rose deserved to be an All-Star this season, praising him publicly at every turn. \u201cDerrick Rose is an All-Star. He is getting to where he wants to go with the ball, and he is picking double teams apart,\u201d Casey said. \u201cGuys are being more comfortable now playing against blitzes and traps, and he is playing like an All-Star\u2026 I\u2019m going to tell everybody, he should be an All-Star, he is an All-Star.\u201d To folks in Chicago he will be treated like one when he returns home for the league\u2019s showcase weekend. K.C. Johnson spent 29 years chronicling the local sports scene for the Chicago Tribune and he was on the Bulls beat for the entirety of Rose\u2019s tenure with the franchise. An Evanston native, Johnson is a lifelong Chicagoan himself, giving him a unique perspective on Rose\u2019s wild ride. \u201cThe thing about it is it was authentic,\u201d said Johnson, now the Bulls Insider for NBC Sports Chicago. \u201cIt was a genuine connection because Derrick not only was fantastic on the court, he also carried a lot of the qualities Chicagoans respect off the court: humble, unassuming, hard-working good teammate, didn\u2019t want a lot of attention on himself.\u201d At his core, he stayed the same before and after the ACL injury that altered the trajectory of his career. Before the ACL, everything was storybook, fairy tale and almost too good to be true, Johnson said. Afterwards, things got a little messy and complicated. \u201cIt was huge [before the ACL] because he was somebody who embraced the responsibility of playing for his hometown team, rather than running from it,\u201d Johnson said. \u201cA lot of players that burden becomes too heavy. Derrick not only embraced it, he enjoyed it. And he did it in a way where he wasn\u2019t beating his chest and shouting it. He was doing it with his play and with the way he comported himself on and off the court.\u201d Perhaps that\u2019s why Thomas, Parker, Richardson, Nunn and so many others swell with pride when describing how important Rose is to the culture of Chicago basketball. \u201cThat\u2019s why his redemption to me is so fun to watch, because every time he comes here, no matter if he\u2019s wearing a Timberwolves uniform or a Pistons uniform, he gets the MVP chants he got when he was playing with the Bulls,\u201d Johnson said. Parker believes Rose is in the midst of authoring yet another intriguing chapter in one of Chicago\u2019s greatest basketball stories. \u201cMy dad has always been the one telling me about our history,\u201d he sad. \u201cI\u2019m talking about all the way back to Cazzie Russell and Jerry Sloan and guys of his era and since then, as well as players before them. We just have a long history to embrace. And all of those guys who come up are going to remember Derrick. They are going to know Derrick. And that\u2019s really special about it, because in a lot of ways he paved the way for everybody from Chicago that\u2019s come after him.\u201d * * * Sekou Smith is a veteran NBA reporter and NBA TV analyst. The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":28698,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/chicago-week-recounting-1988-all-star-weekend","date":"2020-02-13","title":"Legendary star power, embracing of competition highlighted Chicago's last All-Star party","content":" The plane from New York touched down safely at O\u2019Hare Airport. As it taxied to the gate and Rick Welts unbuckled his seat belt, he literally froze from the announcement made over the loudspeaker. \u201cWelcome to Chicago,\u201d the pilot said. \u201cThe wind chill is minus-22.\u201d February is normally sluggish at the welcome center in Chicago because the Yukon is often a balmier option for travelers. This is not a sensible time to frolic on Oak Street Beach or window-shop along Michigan Avenue or see the beasts at the Lincoln Park Zoo who are probably under wraps anyway except for the penguins. But Welts was an NBA vice president on a business trip, prepping for the 1988 edition of All-Star Weekend which was his baby \u2014 he created the concept four years earlier \u2014 and Chicago suddenly was a popular destination in the dead of winter for two reasons: The promise of greatness from Michael Jordan and the guarantee of warmth for a sport played indoors. \u201cThank goodness for both,\u201d said Welts, now the chief operating officer of the Golden State Warriors. As a bonus, Larry Bird was in town to steal the weekend and nearly succeeded. Ask any historian \u2014 better yet, just check the hazy CBS and TBS video and listen to Dick Stockton and Skip Caray \u2014 and you\u2019ll understand why the league\u2019s annual midseason showcase was never more glorious than it was in Sweet Home in \u201988. Listen up: Jordan won the dunk contest in a duel over Dominique Wilkins. Bird proved his mastery of the 3-point shot. And the 1988 All-Star Game was played intensely with Jordan winning MVP before the home fans \u2014 but not before bodies were diving on the floor. Need we say more? That weekend will be forever symbolized by Jordan\u2019s takeoff just inside the free-throw line and Bird giving us the index finger while his money ball was still in the air and then Jordan again raising the trophy on the final day. And the best thing about that weekend, other than thank heavens for overcoats and scarfs, is how it still resonates here on the eve of All-Star\u2019s return to Chicago. Oh, what a different time it was: Jordan hadn\u2019t yet won the first of his six championships with the Bulls, Kareem Abdul-Jabbar made his final All-Star Game, all events were staged inside an old barn named Chicago Stadium which had staircases leading to brick walls, and the first name of the starting center on the West was spelled \u201cAkeem.\u201d The weekend truly belonged to the host city; in addition to the presence of Jordan, it was the birthplace to three All-Stars that year: Isiah Thomas, Mark Aguirre and a star-struck Doc Rivers who couldn\u2019t believe his timing. \u201cI made one All Star team in my career and it\u2019s the one in Chicago,\u201d he said. \u201cI\u2019m from Chicago and I made it with Isiah and Mark. We grew up together and played at 10th Park in Maywood, over by Proviso East, where I went to high school. That was the All Star team to make.\u201d  The NBA then was robust, throbbing with heated rivalries and blessed by legendary players still in their prime, with winning teams in the biggest markets and the league making roots with a new generation of fans. The 80s laid the foundation for what we see in today\u2019s NBA. Best of all, it had Jordan, already making inroads as a marketing Goliath and starving to become the face of the league. \u201cMichael was not from Chicago, but he was the perfect child for Chicago, especially for those two days,\u201d Rivers said. Also, the \u201988 All-Star Weekend was an exhibition in name only. The pride of the players involved, along with their unflinching competitive instinct, made for an intense 48 hours. Rivers said: \u201cWith that generation of players, no one backed down to no one. You respected everyone but when the game started you were trying to go at him. The game was played that way and the weekend was that way, too. All the events on Saturday. It was a competitive weekend.\u201d Welts had the job of coordinating the behind-the-scenes nuts and bolts, a task he was familiar with. In 1984 he and the new commissioner, David Stern, wanted to expand the All-Star experience to involve more players and gain more sponsorship and attract media attention. After the league stole the dunk contest from the ABA, Welts pushed for another skills event and so the 3-point shootout was added. They also created an old-timer\u2019s game but after five too many geezers collapsed from injuries it was eventually and thankfully scrapped. Today, it\u2019s often a chore to get superstars to compete in the Saturday night skills events. In \u201988, it was a cinch; Jordan signed up immediately for a hotly anticipated dunk contest with two-time champ Wilkins among others, and the shootout wasn\u2019t lacking a legendary contestant, either. \u201cGetting Larry in the contest wasn\u2019t an issue because he loved kicking everybody\u2019s ass,\u201d Welts said. \u201cBird\u2019s gamesmanship was a classic moment. He was just bent on crushing people.\u201d Besides, Bird was going for his third straight 3-point contest win. Two years earlier at the inaugural event, he entered the room where the other contestants gathered and famously announced: \u201cWhich one of you is finishing second?\u201d Bird then made 11 straight shots at one point to back up his talk. In 1988, before a back injury stole his mobility, Bird was so cocky and sure about his chances that he refused to remove his warm-up top the entire contest while he shot. But, uh-oh: Bird trailed Dale Ellis by eight points in the final round with only two racks of balls left. As Ellis watched from a few feet away, his expression turning grimmer by the second as Bird came through in the clutch. He swished all five balls from his next-to-last rack. He needed to make the final ball on the final rack to win. Well, with the clock running and the crowd standing and Ellis dreading what was coming next, Bird aimed from the deep corner and released. While the ball was just starting its descent toward the hoop, Bird shot a finger in the air to signify who was No. 1, yet again, trash talking without saying a word. And that was just the appetizer. Wilkins in the 1980s was almost as entertaining as Jordan. The \u201cHuman Highlight Film\u201d , who would finish second in scoring to Jordan in 1987-88 with 30.7 points a game, was a must-see performer, especially when he had the ball on the break with nobody standing between himself and the rim. In those situations, photographers reached for their tools and fans rose from their seats. Wilkins was an exceptionally stylish dunker, who did so with force and creativity. What was particularly interesting is how neither Wilkins nor Jordan dodged each other in \u201988, not like prize fighters. Both Jordan (who won in 1987 in Seattle ) and Wilkins (the winner in 1985 in Indianapolis ) each already had a dunk contest win apiece in their pockets. Yet they put their reps and egos on the line for a title that mostly paid out bragging rights, and with the marquee set, the dunk contest generated more fan interest than the game the next day. \u201cI wanted to beat him as much as he wanted to beat me,\u201d Wilkins said. \u201cMichael and I were gonna put on a show. You didn\u2019t have one guy who was definitely better than the other. You had the two best dunkers in the game. No matter who won, everyone was getting their money\u2019s worth.\u201d Each easily made the final and had three dunks in the championship round. Wilkins dunked on a lob off the backboard for a maximum 50 points. Jordan followed with a between-the-legs beauty for 50. Jordan\u2019s next dunk was for 47 points. Wilkins did him one better for 48. On his final dunk, Wilkins executed a windmill that he said was his best of the day. But, in voting that conjured up some of those notorious Chicago political elections of the past, Wilkins was awarded just 45 points. Jordan had a chance. He missed the next dunk, but the rules allowed for one more. Getting a nod from Julius Erving, who soared from the free-throw line to win the 1976 ABA dunk title, Jordan sprinted from the distant end of the floor. His right foot straddled the line \u2014 a mere technicality \u2014 and in order to gain more hang time to reach the rim, Jordan double-clutched in mid-air. The dunk was successful, the crowd roared and the judges\u2019 cards all read 10 points each for a perfect 50. Jordan was sympathetic to Wilkins and thought the final dunk by the Hawks forward was worth more than 45. A few years earlier Wilkins lost the dunk-off to 5-foot-7 Spud Webb in Webb\u2019s hometown of Dallas, and so this was more of the same. Wilkins laughs about it today and likes to say he won four dunk contests but only has two trophies to show for it. \u201cIt\u2019s always stacked against you when you\u2019re in that situation,\u201d he said \u201cThat\u2019s to be expected. It would\u2019ve been the same if the All-Star Game was in Atlanta. Do I think I won? Yeah. But no regrets.\u201d \u201cYou know, me and Mike, we\u2019ve never talked about that contest, ever,\u201d Wilkins said. \u201cEven to this day. It\u2019s kind of an unspoken thing. We know what we did that night. We know what we brought to the game. We killed it. Mike\u2019s dunks were incredible. And I thought mines were incredible too. He said later that he would\u2019ve given me the trophy, but the contest was in Chicago and they would\u2019ve had a riot had he done that. Well, today I\u2019d tell him to just give me the $100,000 he won and we can call it even.\u201d Jordan\u2019s competitive thirst was still evident the next day; in his greed, Jordan wanted the total weekend to be a ringing endorsement for him. At halftime, his team, the East, was up 60-54 and that was too close for Jordan. He sensed his fellow stars were letting their desire dip a bit.  \u201cMike came into the locker room,\u201d Rivers said, \u201cand he went off. He said, \u2018We ain\u2019t losing, not on my floor, not in my place. Ain\u2019t gonna happen.\u2019 Then he turned to Mike Fratello, who was coaching us, and told him that in the second half, only play the guys who want to play. Jordan knew who the competitors were on the team.\u201d Rivers noticed an immediate uptick and how Fratello did as instructed. Only those who were willing to scrap and dive and hustle were on the floor for any extended time. That included Rivers, who owed his All-Star appearance for being that very same type of player for the Hawks that season. \u201cWe were picking up full court on defense in the second half,\u201d Rivers said. \u201cShow me another All-Star Game where you\u2019ve seen that. Well, at least you don\u2019t see that as much today when the scores are pushing 200 points for each team. That might\u2019ve been the last great competitive game where it wasn\u2019t a show, it was straight competition.\u201d Jordan scored 40 points in the 138-133 East win. There were no claims of ballot-stuffing this time. Jordan was an easy choice for MVP and the weekend officially belonged to him, if only because he sparked a pair of intense competitions and caused everyone involved to elevate their level of play. \u201cThe game was bragging rights plus you got that paycheck for winning,\u201d Wilkins said. \u201cWe argued with the refs and everything. We wanted to win, and it wasn\u2019t easy because you had all that great talent around you and on the other side of the floor. The tables were evenly set.\u201d It is now 2020 and Lake Michigan still freezes in winter, although the temps might be merciful this time around. Chicago Stadium turned to dust many years ago and the big events this year will be held in the United Center with entertainers sitting courtside, hyped player introductions and a starry halftime show. The All-Star Weekend returns to Sweet Home on a much grander scale in every which way, with a different cast of characters. If Chicago is lucky, the passion of 1988 will be duplicated, although that will be tricky since the only shot of Michael Jordan soaring this time will be taken with cameras aimed at his statue in the United Center lobby. \u201cThat weekend helped Michael\u2019s legacy,\u201d Welts said. \u201cThe whole thing was on a silver platter for him, and he took every advantage that he possibly could.\u201d * * * Shaun Powell has covered the NBA for more than 25 years. Hudson\u2019s performance will open the night ahead of player introductions for the 69th NBA All-Star Game on Sunday, Feb. 16 at the United Center, airing live at 8 p.m. ET on TNT.  The league also unveiled an All-Star lineup of performers that will join previously announced three-time GRAMMY winner and Chicago native Chance The Rapper, Golden Globe, Emmy, Oscar and three-time GRAMMY winner and Chicago native Common, and Chance\u2019s brother and fellow rap artist Taylor Bennett, throughout the weekend.  Before All-Star team introductions, Common will welcome fans to Chicago with a moving narrative about what basketball means to the city. Prior to tip-off, Chicagoan and 10-time GRAMMY winner Chaka Khan will sing the U.S. national anthem and country music singer-songwriter Tenille Arts will sing the Canadian national anthem. At halftime, Chance The Rapper will take the stage with five-time GRAMMY winner Lil Wayne, 2020 GRAMMY winner DJ Khaled and GRAMMY-nominated Migos rapper Quavo for a star-studded performance of their chart-topping hits. During State Farm All-Star Saturday Night on Feb. 15, Golden Globe-, GRAMMY- and Primetime Emmy Award-winning rapper, singer and actress Queen Latifah will be joined by Chicago youth for a special performance of Stevie Wonder\u2019s classic song \u201cLove\u2019s in Need of Love Today.\u201d The evening will tip off at 8 p.m. ET on TNT. On Friday, Feb. 14, the Chicago Children\u2019s Choir will sing the U.S. national anthem in advance of NBA Rising Stars, which begins at 9 p.m. ET on TNT. As previously announced, Taylor Bennett will perform at halftime. # # # About the Artists Chaka Khan Chaka Khan is one of the world\u2019s most gifted and celebrated music icons. A singer, songwriter, actor, author, philanthropist, entrepreneur and activist, Chaka Khan has influenced generations of music creators during her four decades as an artist. A 10-time GRAMMY Award winner, she has the rare ability to sing in a number of musical genres, including R&B, pop, rock, gospel, country, world music and classical. During her legendary career, Chaka has released 22 albums and racked up 10 No. 1 songs on the Billboard charts, seven RIAA-certified gold singles and 10 RIA-certified gold and platinum albums. Chaka\u2019s recordings have resulted in more than 2,000 catalog song placements. Chicago Children\u2019s Choir Chicago Children\u2019s Choir (Josephine Lee, President and Artistic Director) is a non-profit organization that inspires and unites youth from diverse backgrounds to become global citizens through music. Founded in Hyde Park in direct response to the Civil Rights Movement in 1956, CCC has grown from one choir into a vast network of in-school and after-school programs serving 5,200 students across the city of Chicago. Learn more at ccchoir.org. DJ Khaled Since launching his career two decades ago, GRAMMY winner, multiplatinum artist, music industry visionary, innovative influencer, mogul and mega-producer DJ Khaled has sold 18 million singles and 5 million albums and generated more than 3 billion streams. His discography encompasses eight gold singles, five platinum singles and two double-platinum singles. He landed back-to-back No. 1 debuts on the Billboard Top 200 with the gold-selling \u201cMajor Key\u201d in 2016 and platinum-certified GRAMMY Award-nominated \u201cGrateful\u201d in 2017. The latter boasted the sextuple-platinum Billboard Hot 100 No. 1 \u201cI\u2019m The One\u201d (feat. Justin Bieber, Quavo, Chance the Rapper, & Lil Wayne) and quadruple-platinum \u201cWild Thoughts\u201d (feat. Rihanna & Bryson Tiller). His 12th full-length album, \u201cFather of Asahd,\u201d was the \u201cNo. 1 Most-Streamed Record\u201d upon release in 2019, achieved a gold certification from the RIAA and marked his third straight Top 2 debut on the Billboard Top 200. He also runs We the Best Music Group, is a New York Times bestselling author and served as a judge on Fox\u2019s top-rated 2018 smash \u201cThe Four.\u201d Jennifer Hudson A two-time GRAMMY winning recording artist, Academy Award-winning actress and best-selling author, Jennifer Hudson is an extraordinary talent. From humble beginnings, Hudson began singing in a small Chicago church where, week after week, she brought the congregation to its feet. From there, she\u2019s gone on to worldwide acclaim. Hudson is currently in the final days of production on Liesl Tommy\u2019s film, RESPECT, in which she stars as Aretha Franklin alongside Forrest Whitaker, Mary J. Blige and Marlon Wayans. The film for which Hudson was handpicked by the late Ms. Franklin will hit theatres Oct. 9, 2020. Prior to that, she returned for her second season on NBC\u2019s \u201cThe Voice\u201d alongside Adam Levine, Blake Shelton, and Kelly Clarkson and for her third season of \u201cThe Voice UK\u201d alongside coaches Tom Jones, will.i.am, and Olly Murs. Hudson\u2019s song, \u201cI\u2019ll Fight,\u201d written by Grammy-winning songwriter Diane Warren for the critically acclaimed documentary, \u201cRBG,\u201d was nominated for a 2019 Academy Award, where Hudson performed. In 2007, Hudson won an Academy Award for her role as Effie in the smash hit \u201cDreamgirls,\u201d an adaptation of the Broadway musical. Her breakout film performance also garnered a Golden Globe Award, a Screen Actors Guild Award, a BAFTA and an NAACP Image Award. A five-time nominee, Hudson won her first Grammy in 2009 for Best R&B Album and again for Best Musical Theatre Album in 2017. Lil Wayne The five-time GRAMMY-winning multiplatinum rap icon, Young Money Entertainment founder, acclaimed author, skater, philanthropist, and history-making superstar has left an indelible tattoo on the culture. By 2020, he cemented his legacy forever as \u201cone of the best-selling artists of all time,\u201d tallying sales in excess of 100 million records worldwide with 15 million albums and 37 million digital tracks sold in the U.S. alone. Not to mention, he garnered 11 BET Awards, four Billboard Music Awards, two MTV VMAs, and eight NCAAP Image Awards. He emerged as \u201cthe first male artist to surpass Elvis Presley with the most entries on the Billboard Hot 100,\u201d logging a staggering 109 entries. With a discography highlighted by two consecutive No. 1 debuts on the Billboard Top 200 with 2008\u2019s triple-platinum three-time GRAMMY-winning opus \u201cTha Carter III\u201d and its double-platinum 2011 successor \u201cTha Carter IV,\u201d he completed the series with \u201cTha Carter V\u201d in 2018. Not only did it clinch No. 1 on the Billboard Top 200, but it also achieved \u201cthe second-largest streaming week for an album in history.\u201d Every song on the tracklisting charted on the Billboard Hot 100 as he became \u201cthe first artist two debut songs in the top 5.\u201d It all bulldozed the way for his thirteenth full-length, Funeral, a year later. Simultaneously, Wayne owns and operates Young Money Entertainment, which ignited the careers of two of the most successful artists ever, Drake and Nicki Minaj. The committed philanthropist also founded The One Family Foundation too. Quavo Rapper, singer, songwriter, actor, producer, director and \u2018HUNCHO\u2019 of the GRAMMY-nominated group Migos, Quavo is one of the singular voices of hip hop\u2019s new generation. His debut solo album, \u201cQuavo Huncho,\u201d on Quality Control Music \/ Motown \/ Capitol and with breakout singles \u201cWorkin Me\u201d and \u201cBubblegum,\u201d came on the heels of Migos\u2019 \u201cCulture II,\u201d their monumental album and second consecutive No. 1 on the Billboard Top 200 Chart. In 2020, Quavo continues to deliver on the promise that Migos are the culture of this generation. With his songwriting prowess, Quavo has contributed to two No. 1 hits on Billboard\u2019s Hot 100 chart, including DJ Khaled\u2019s \u201cI\u2019m The One\u201d featuring Justin Bieber, Chance The Rapper and Lil Wayne, nine Top 10 hits and 46 songs on the chart in total. His collaborative album \u201cHuncho Jack, Jack Huncho\u201d with Travis Scott debuted at No. 3 on the Billboard Top 200 in December 2017. Quavo has also collaborated with high-profile artists like Beyonc\u00e9 and Jay-Z on their smash \u201cApe Shit,\u201d as well as Kanye West and others across the spectrum including Liam Payne, Halsey, Calvin Harris, Camilla Cabello, Bebe Rexha and many more. In 2018, Quavo toured across the country with Migos and Drake on the sold-out \u201cAubrey and the Three Migos Tour.\u201d He also appeared on Fox\u2019s TV show \u201cStar\u201d and played in the 2018 and 2019 NBA All-Star Celebrity Games, winning MVP in 2018. 2020 marks his third consecutive year on the court. Queen Latifah Queen Latifah, a musician, television and film actress, label president, author and entrepreneur, became the first hip hop artist to be crowned with a star on the Hollywood Walk of Fame on Jan. 4, 2006, and is one of music\u2019s most well-respected rappers. From her groundbreaking 1989 debut, \u201cAll Hail the Queen,\u201d to her bold foray into R&B, Latifah continues to define what a woman in the music industry should be. She has earned six GRAMMY nominations as well as a GRAMMY Award for Best Solo Rap Performance. She has earned numerous accolades for her work as an actress in both film and television, including a Golden Globe nomination and a Screen Actors Guild (SAG) Award nomination for her portrayal of Mama Morton in the feature musical \u201cChicago\u201d; an Emmy Award nomination, a Golden Globe award and a SAG Award for her role in the cable film \u201cLife Support\u201d; and an Emmy Award nomination, Golden Globe nomination and a SAG Award for her role in the cable film \u201cBessie,\u201d which she also produced. In addition, Queen Latifah and her business partner Shakim Compere formed the production company Flavor Unit Entertainment, which has produced numerous projects since its inception. Tenille Arts Born and raised in the small prairie town of Weyburn, Saskatchewan, Tenille Arts picked up the guitar and penned her first song at age 13 and has been performing across the U.S. and Canada ever since. She made her first appearance on \u201cThe Bachelor\u201d in January 2018 singing her original song \u201cMoment of Weakness.\u201d The performance resulted in a record deal with powerhouse indie label Reviver Records. She made her second appearance on the show in January of 2019 performing \u201cI Hate This.\u201d The song hit No. 1 on both the U.S. and Canadian iTunes Country Charts and No. 9 U.S. iTunes all-genre following the episode. Her third appearance on \u201cThe Bachelor\u201d preceded the release of her new album \u201cLove, Heartbreak, & Everything In Between\u201d in January of 2020. Tenille\u2019s music has tallied over 51 million lifetime streams. Streaming giant Pandora named her one of their new artists to watch, and CMT included her in their \u201cTen Must-See Indies You Need to Know,\u201d a video discovery program that spotlights rising independent artists and established artists from independent labels. Rolling Stone named Tenille one of their \u201c10 New Country Artists You Need To Know,\u201d calling her \u201cAn edgy, Fearless-era Taylor Swift with crystalline vocals; ideal for hopeless romantics who are fire signs,\u201d and PopCulture named her one of their \u201cCountry Artists to Watch in 2018.\u201d Tenille made her Grand Ole Opry debut on Sept. 21, 2019 and performed at the Grand Ole Opry at the historic Ryman Auditorium on Jan. 3, 2020. National television appearances include the TODAY Show, the Radio Disney Music Awards and singing the Canadian National Anthem at Game 3 of the 2019 NBA finals.
