That never happened before.\u201d The Mavericks are 32-22 and No. 6 in the Western Conference, but are just two games behind the Houston Rockets for the No. 5 seed."},{"id":28751,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/chicago-week-basketball-bonds","date":"2020-02-12","title":"The bond between players from Chicago runs deep","content":" Right arm propped against a pillar for balance, Miami Heat rookie guard Kendrick Nunn worked slowly through a one-legged rehab routine. All of the sudden, the right knee buckled and he belted out a chuckle. Telling Patrick Beverley stories often elicits such a response. \u201cThat\u2019s my guy 100%,\u201d Nunn said. \u201cThat\u2019s big bro.\u201d Nunn figured that out almost immediately on the day he met Beverley for the first time during a summer workout at the University of Illinois at Chicago. Dribbling through drills to tighten his handle, Nunn could hear raucous yelling coming from the other side of the court. \u201cWe ain\u2019t even met yet,\u201d Nunn recalled, laughing. \u201cIt was funny as hell. Pat was like, \u2018Yo, you standing up that high young fella, I\u2019mma take that [expletive]. You better get low.\u2019\u201d  That first morsel of knowledge serves as one of several that Nunn has insatiably gobbled up as the baby of the crew of NBA players representing the city of Chicago. With All-Star 2020 taking place in Chicago this week, some of the city\u2019s native sons described their tight-knit relationships around the league, and what makes basketball in their hometown so special. From Dwyane Wade to Derrick Rose, there\u2019s no denying that several Chicagoans continue to put on for their city. It\u2019s why Chicagoans like former Memphis Grizzlies forward Tony Allen, from the city\u2019s West Side, unequivocally back Anthony Davis\u2019 assertions that Chicago is the Mecca of basketball . Mention Chicago basketball to Allen, and his eyes light up. He becomes nearly breathless by the end of discussing what he believes is the city\u2019s pastime, despite its long, storied history in professional football. \u201cThere\u2019s so many hoopers,\u201d Allen told NBA.com. \u201cThe list goes on and on. We can go all the way back, man, Isiah Thomas, Mark Aguirre. Shawn Marion, Mike Finley, Tim Hardaway, Antoine Walker, myself, Will Bynum, Quentin Richardson, Bobby Simmons, who else? These Chicago cats, man. \u2026Hey, I agree with A.D. \u201cDon\u2019t forget about our infamous documentary story, the Arthur Agee story (Hoop Dreams), man,\u201d Allen said. \u201cLet\u2019s not forget we were the first ones to put reality TV on the basketball side of things. Real content, man. \u2026 I just think that you\u2019ve got to understand why A.D. is saying that. Come on, man. A lot of people would beg to differ in New York. But then again, yo, we\u2019ve got a basketball culture, too.\u201d But what makes it special? The answer varies among Chicagoans playing in the NBA. But there\u2019s one seemingly universal description. \u201cThe toughness,\u201d said Timberwolves forward Evan Turner, a native of the West Side of Chicago. \u201cYou\u2019ve got to be really, really tough to make it out of there. You hear about the pros, but there are so many people that didn\u2019t make it that are killing elsewhere that went on and became legends at certain universities or overseas. That type of toughness is second to none. When you feel like you can make it in Chicago, you can play anywhere.\u201d Added Nunn, who hails from the South Side: \u201cIf you look at all the Chicago players, we have a different toughness about how we play. I think that\u2019s just in our blood, just the way we carry ourselves being from Chicago.\u201d That\u2019s primarily because of the intense rivalries within the city at high schools and on local playgrounds. Beverley, Rose and Duke associate head coach Jon Scheyer grew up admiring the Chicagoans that came before them such as Hardaway, Walker, Marion, Simmons, Allen and Bynum.  But they also came up in Chicago as fierce rivals with Rose leading Simeon Career Academy, Beverley starring at Marshall, while Scheyer played at Glenbrook North. Turner, who calls fellow Chicagoan Iman Shumpert one of his closest friends, started playing with Beverley at age \u201c11 or 12,\u201d in Oak Park, Ill., he said. Back then, and even right now, regardless of all the talent permeating the national hoops scene, the focus in Chicago was on what was going on locally. Forget about trying to see how you fare against a player from Los Angeles or New York. The players in Chicago care mostly about establishing dominance at home first, they say. \u201cEven though there\u2019s a lot of talent around the country, [we would be] be arrogant in the sense of if you went to an All-Star camp or somewhere else, you\u2019d want to play versus each other or we\u2019d be focusing on trying to play at nationals versus whatever other Chicago team we saw,\u201d Turner said. \u201cThat\u2019s how we competed; like worrying about who ran the city and who was really holding it down and doing their thing. \u2026There\u2019s a lot of Chicago talent in the city, and I really believe if you can play in Chicago, you can play anywhere. \u201cIt\u2019s hard to even explain it, bro,\u201d Turner added. \u201cEvery weekend wherever you went, you had to really strap in. I remember telling people and they thought I was joking. But growing up on the West Side, the kids I played versus in the alley were some of the toughest kids I\u2019ve played against. \u2026The playground battles, going to Franklin Park, it\u2019s like everybody you knew was getting busy. So, if you were the man at your school, there were a couple of kids walking that hall where on any given day they could give you some problems. If you go to a different school, Proviso East or Proviso West up the street, you\u2019ll hear, \u2018This [other] dude is an animal. This dude is giving out pure hell.\u2019 There are so many guards I grew up playing with or playing versus. That\u2019s what pops in my mind when it comes to Chicago talent; not the obvious players, but some of the local stars. It\u2019s just unreal. There was just that much talent, man. And it made you better. If you made it out of there, you just knew you could go on and do great things.\u201d Nunn and Sacramento Kings forward Jabari Parker met in the sixth grade \u201con the AAU scene,\u201d according to Nunn. They quickly became friends, despite the fact they \u201chad always battled,\u201d according to Parker. They later formed what Parker called \u201ca brotherhood bond,\u201d which would help the duo rattle off four straight state championships at Simeon Career Academy, Rose\u2019s alma mater. Parker received all the accolades, as he was named National High School Player of the Year by both Gatorade and McDonald\u2019s.  But he\u2019s quick to point out Nunn\u2019s contributions. \u201cPeople give me a lot of credit for four state championships, but I can\u2019t take that credit myself,\u201d Parker said. \u201cI had a great team. Kendrick was one of those guys where I would have never gotten that far if it wasn\u2019t for him. And other guys, too. But I always wanted to bring up Kendrick when it came to my legacy because it should never be me alone. It should be him right there with me.\u201d Nunn was definitely there for Parker when injuries started to derail what was supposed to be a promising NBA career. There was a time when Parker drew comparisons to players such as Carmelo Anthony and Paul Pierce when he first entered the league, but injuries have prevented the six-year veteran from living up to the hype. \u201cKendrick was always there for me,\u201d Parker said. \u201cI\u2019ve always been there for him. Shoot man, anything he needed from me, I\u2019ve got him. And he\u2019s got my back.\u201d Beverley shares similar sentiments regarding former MVP Rose, who has dealt with his fair share of injuries since coming into the league. \u201cHe\u2019s like my brother,\u201d Beverley told NBA.com. \u201cAnything I ask for, he gives it; vice-versa. Whether it\u2019s a car to drive if I\u2019m in his town for a week or if his girl flies to L.A. and [his team hasn\u2019t] gotten there yet, then she comes to my house until they land. It\u2019s just a family thing. We support each other, always have each other\u2019s back. If someone\u2019s going through surgery, we\u2019re right there with him. It\u2019s that Chicago love.\u201d  Parker and Nunn grew up idolizing Rose and Beverley. Parker watched Rose play for the first time in person as an eighth grader. Nunn refers to both Rose and Beverley as \u201cbig bros that have taken me under their wing.\u201d You can also count Pelicans center Jahlil Okafor, who played high school ball at Whitney Young in Chicago, as part of the city\u2019s NBA brotherhood as well. Parker says he \u201clooked up\u201d to Turner growing up. So, when they became teammates in Atlanta over the summer, it was \u201ca dream come true for me.\u201d \u201cNo matter where I go in my career, I\u2019m always paying homage to the guys I looked up to, and [Turner] was one of them,\u201d Parker said. \u201cWe\u2019re [all] extremely close because unlike L.A., L.A. has so many other cities. It\u2019s so spread out. You live hours away from a dude. In New York, it\u2019s like private schools. But then you go to Connecticut for a prep school or Rhode Island or Providence. It\u2019s like that. And for Chicago, it\u2019s like we\u2019re so close together because the city is only so big. We all had the same coaches growing up. We\u2019ve played at the same camps. The Chicago culture is very tight knit.\u201d \u201cSome kids come up to you and be like, \u2018I remember when I was a shorty, I watched you play,\u2019\u201d Turner said. \u201cJabari has come up to me and been like, \u2018Yo, I remember when I was 12 years old and you went up against D. Rose at Northwestern, and that was like the coldest game I ever saw.\u2019 I remember being at the crib and seeing Jabari having stuff on smash, and DM\u2019ing him like, \u2018Yo, keep doing your thing, keep playing on.\u2019\u201d While players such as Beverley and Davis don\u2019t typically spend the offseason back in Chicago over the summer, many of the players from the city do. They also maintain a large group chat in which they\u2019re constantly discussing where the next pick-up game is taking place or finding ways to link up to hang. \u201cWe\u2019re trying to make sure we can get some runs at UIC,\u201d Turner said. \u201cGuys are always trying to make sure we try to get back and play or see where each guy is. [Suns center Frank] Kaminsky, Shump, [Cavaliers forward] Alfonzo McKinnie \u2014 he grew up in my hood \u2014 Jacob Pullen, they\u2019re all on the chat.\u201d As Nunn finishes up his pregame rehab exercises, he takes a seat in front of his locker to ponder the importance of basketball in the city of Chicago. \u201cIt\u2019s used as a tool to get out of the streets of Chicago,\u201d Nunn said. \u201cSo, it\u2019s been huge for us, and it continues to grow with our youth. When the kids play basketball, it stops the violence. And even though all of us in the NBA are from different areas and parts of Chicago, this is what brings us together. We\u2019re in the same profession. We love to do the same things. This just connects us all.\u201d * * * Michael C. Wright is a senior writer for NBA.com. A bevy of games before the All-Star break It\u2019s hard to believe, perhaps, but there are just two more days until NBA All-Star 2020 in Chicago gets rolling. Tonight\u2019s 11-game slate has some prime matchups \u2014 which we\u2019ll get into shortly \u2014 but consider this a notice to park it in front of the TV or your device and soak up everything there is on tonight between ESPN and NBA League Pass. Although there are still two games on tomorrow night, this is the last night of 10 or more games until Feb. 28.  2. Raptors seek 16th straight win If you haven\u2019t heard, this 15-game win streak Toronto is on is not only the longest in team history, but the longest in the history of the city across any sport. At 40-14, the Raptors have reached the 40-win mark before the All-Star break for the third straight year. Tonight, they can keep all that rolling and sweep the season series with the Brooklyn Nets ( 7:30 ET, League Pass ) to boot. Toronto has had its depth and roster versatility on display throughout this run (and, to be honest, all season long) and hasn\u2019t had much trouble vs. Brooklyn lately either, winning 18 of the last 19 games between the teams. 3. In losing their last six games, the Indiana Pacers have gone from being No. 5 (with a realistic shot at the No. 3 spot ) to No. 6 in the East . Closing out games has been an issue for Indiana lately and hosting Milwaukee next ( 7:30 ET, ESPN ) would hardly seem like the cure for the Pacers\u2019 ills. The Bucks are 11-0 against Central Division foes this season, have won nine straight games by double digits and at 46-7 are tied for the fifth-best start to a season in NBA history.  4. Doncic ready to return For the past seven games, the Dallas Mavericks have tried to make do without All-Star Luka Doncic in the mix as he recovers from an ankle injury . The Mavs went 3-4 during that stretch, picked up a new swingman (Michael Kidd-Gilchrist) and saw Kristaps Porzingis flash his former All-Star skills at times. Barring a setback today, though, Doncic said he is expecting to suit up against the Kings ( 8:30 ET, League Pass ). The Lakers have been dominant against the West\u2019s other playoff teams , but the Nuggets are one of four playoff-contending squads to score a win against L.A. The Heat could use this win as well as they are 5-7 against the West\u2019s current playoff teams and were swept by the Clippers and Lakers this season. In their annual rankings, Forbes said franchise values across the league have risen 14% over the past year to reach an average value of $2.12 billion. The Los Angeles Lakers come in second in the rankings with a value of $4.4 billion. Rounding out the top five are the Golden State Warriors ($4.3 billion), Chicago Bulls ($3.2 billion) and Boston Celtics ($3.1 billion). The reigning champion Toronto Raptors saw the largest year-over-year increase with their franchise value rising 25% to $2.1 billion."},{"id":28749,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/charleston-grant-riller-draft-feature","date":"2020-02-11","title":"Charleston's Grant Riller stuffing the stat sheet and catching attention of NBA scouts","content":"On the road recruiting post man Kerry Blackshear during the summer of 2014, then-Clemson assistant Earl Grant kept noticing one of Blackshear\u2019s AAU teammates, a scrawny guard named Grant Riller. In those days, Riller was 6-foot-1 and, on a good day, 160 pounds or so, and while Grant wasn\u2019t sure Riller was physically imposing enough to play for Clemson and handle the rigors of the Atlantic Coast Conference, he knew he was a baller. So Grant began calling friends who were assistant coaches at mid-major schools and letting them know Riller \u2014 a talented kid with high-major skill but a mid-major body \u2014 was out there. After about two weeks of sounding the alarm on Riller, Grant abruptly stopped making phone calls on his behalf. That\u2019s because, after a sudden coaching change at the College of Charleston in early August, Grant, who had worked for respected coaches Gregg Marshall (Winthrop, Wichita State) and Brad Brownell at Clemson, was deemed ready to run his own program. After landing the Charleston job in early September, Grant hoped his promotion of Riller hadn\u2019t been too effective, because now he was at a place where Riller\u2019s talent could overcome his lack of size. By this time, Riller had narrowed his choices to FIU and Cleveland State, but once Grant and new assistant coach Quinton Ferrell became involved in Riller\u2019s recruitment, and after he got a look at Charleston \u2014 the city and the school \u2014 he was hooked. \u201cTiming is everything,\u201d Grant says. It didn\u2019t hurt that Riller\u2019s Orlando, Florida homeboy, point guard Joe Chealey, was already on Charleston\u2019s roster. Chealey would go on to sign a two-way contract with the Charlotte Hornets in 2018. \u201cHe helped make the transition easier,\u201d Riller says. So easy, in fact, Grant and Ferrell were stunned at how quickly Riller\u2019s talent translated to the college level. It was the summer of 2015, and when Riller showed up on campus along with Jarrell Brantley \u2014 a forward who was a second-round pick of the Indiana Pacers in the 2019 NBA Draft and later traded to the Utah Jazz \u2014 the Charleston coaches were excited about the possibilities of their first recruiting class. \u201cWith Grant, it was just like \u2018wow\u2019 \u201d says Ferrell, who became Riller\u2019s position coach and is now the head coach at Presbyterian. \u201cWe obviously knew Grant was good, but to see it translate to the college level right away. \u2026 He scored the ball so easily. We thought this kid\u2019s going to be freshman of the year [in the Colonial Athletic Association].\u201d Further evidence of Riller\u2019s ability came in the fall, just weeks before the season began, when the Cougars were playing Clemson in a closed scrimmage. Riller had scored 20 points against the Tigers with seven minutes to go. And then a freak accident happened. \u201cThe Clemson player was dribbling to the sideline,\u201d Riller says. \u201cI was guarding the ball. But he faked a timeout and tried to drive. My foot got stuck on the floor. I wasn\u2019t expecting it and I got caught lagging. I knew something was wrong right away.\u201d Riller had torn the ACL in his left knee. The diagnosis and realization he would miss the entire season was crushing, but Riller quickly realized a year on the sidelines might wind up being to his advantage. \u201cIt was a blessing in disguise,\u201d Riller says. \u201cThere had been a lot of questions about my weight at the time, and whether I could play at this level. The year gave me an opportunity to work on my body. It gave me an opportunity to learn the game. Joe [Chealey] had torn his Achilles a couple of weeks earlier, so I had someone to go through the redshirt year with.\u201d Once Riller got on the floor in 2016, he made up for lost time. Having added two inches and 20 pounds of muscle to his frame, he started 29 games, averaged 13.1 points and made the CAA All-Rookie team. The next season, now packing 195 pounds, Riller averaged 18.6 points, racked up four games of 30 points or more, and along with Chealey and Brantley, helped lead Charleston back to the NCAA tournament for the first time in 19 years. A year ago, after Chealey departed for the NBA, Riller took over as point guard. He led the Cougars in assists but still managed to increase his scoring average to 21.9 points a game, 20th in the nation. This season, Riller is on pace to match those statistics, and he\u2019s also starting to turn up on mock NBA Draft boards. Choose a metric and Riller\u2019s accomplishments are impressive. You want traditional stats? Riller is averaging 21.8 points while shooting 51.2% from the field, 38.7% from 3-point range and 85.2% from the free-throw line. He\u2019s already attempted 162 free throws. At a glance, that speaks to Riller\u2019s remarkable ability to finish at the rim and either score or get fouled. His ever-improving skill from the 3-point line has made him one of the more difficult covers in the college game. Power conference coaches would attest to that. \u201cGrant Riller isn\u2019t only one of the best mid-major players in the country,\u201d Oklahoma State coach Mike Boynton, Jr. said after Riller dropped 16 points, eight assists, four rebounds and three steals on the Cowboys. \u201cHe\u2019s one of the best guards at any level, period. He could start for any team in the Big 12.\u201d If advanced metrics are your pleasure, Synergy rates Riller excellent in overall offense (1.055 points per possession), excellent as a pick and roll ball handler (1.103 ppp), excellent in isolation (1.115 ppp), excellent in jump shots off the dribble (1.021 ppp) and very good at catch and shoot (1.138 ppp). Riller is a stat sheet stuffer who last month put together the school\u2019s first triple double (20 points, 10 rebounds, 10 assists against Northeastern). He averages 5.0 rebounds, impressive for a point guard, along with 4.1 assists and 1.5 steals. But numbers alone don\u2019t tell Riller\u2019s story. \u201cHe\u2019s the ultimate competitor,\u201d Grant says. \u201cOne thing people don\u2019t talk about, but he\u2019s won our Purple Heart Award for taking charges. This year, so far, he\u2019s taken 11 charges in conference play. That says a lot about how much he wants to win.\u201d Riller\u2019s basketball IQ and work ethic also don\u2019t show up on stat sheets, but they\u2019ve combined to give him a chance, like Chealey and Brantley before him, to make an NBA roster. \u201cHe\u2019s always had the natural ability to score,\u201d Ferrell says. \u201cBut his ability to read the game, his playmaking, his ability to pick his spots. \u2026 He\u2019s gone to another level with his playmaking. He\u2019s an underrated passer. People at the next level will be very surprised at his passing ability. He\u2019ll shoot the crap out of the ball when he goes to NBA workouts. From an offensive standpoint, there are no holes in his game.\u201d NBA scouts have been regular visitors to Charleston practices and games the last three years, so Riller has caught their attention. \u201cI mean, they really like him,\u201d Grant says. Riller isn\u2019t letting NBA talk interfere with his senior season. He loved experiencing the NCAA tournament \u2014 Riller played 40 minutes and scored 16 points against Auburn in 2018 \u2014 and would love to lead his team back there. Grant won\u2019t begin to focus on Riller\u2019s NBA chances until after the season, but he doesn\u2019t hesitate to envision what Riller might be able to accomplish at the next level. \u201cHe can play both guard spots,\u201d Grant says. \u201cI think he\u2019s going to be better as a point guard. He\u2019s got great feel for slipping through ball screens, a great feel for passing. He\u2019s got good size for his position. But if a team needs him to be a guy that can make five 3s in a game and defend his position, he can do that, too.\u201d Like any young player, Riller grew up watching NBA games and dreaming about one day playing in the league. \u201cA dream, sure,\u201d Riller says. \u201cBut you never really know if it can come true. As the years have progressed and I kept working on my game, I think I\u2019ve put myself in pretty good position. I\u2019ll keep working as hard as I can. And if it happens one day, I\u2019ll be forever grateful.\u201d * * * Chris Dortch is the editor of the Blue Ribbon College Basketball Yearbook . "},{"id":28754,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/kia-mvp-mailbag-feb-11","date":"2020-02-11","title":"Kia MVP Ladder mailbag: Raptors keep rolling behind coach Nick Nurse","content":" Editor\u2019s note: The weekly Kia MVP Ladder will resume on Feb. 21, but there will not be one on Feb. 14 because of NBA All-Star 2020. * * * They don\u2019t do championship hangovers in Toronto. Raptors coach Nick Nurse has made sure of it with the way he\u2019s pushing buttons for his team this season. Their winning streak ( 15 games and counting as of this writing) is as impressive as anything that\u2019s been done outside of Milwaukee this season. The fact Toronto has done this with a shorthanded roster and without the assistance of the reigning Finals MVP makes this encore season all the more impressive. They\u2019ve got two All-Stars in Pascal Siakam and Kyle Lowry, a ridiculously deep roster and perhaps the best player development program in the league right now. What they also have is the frontrunner for Coach of the Year in Nurse, who has silenced any doubters who thought the 2019 title run was strictly some Kawhi Leonard production and everyone else serving as bit players. Siakam is an All-Star Game starter and has developed into a legitimate Kia MVP candidate under Nurse\u2019s tutelage. Raptors boss Masai Ujiri, a perennial contender for Executive of the Year honors, clearly did the right thing in handing the keys to the team over to Nurse when he did. And Nurse has been spot on in driving his team to the longest winning streak in team and Canadian pro sports history. \u201cI think we\u2019re [in] pretty good spirits, obviously, and we\u2019re trying to keep the streak alive,\u201d Nurse said before Monday\u2019s game vs. Minnesota. \u201cI think that\u2019s helpful to get us to the break. I don\u2019t know, I hadn\u2019t really thought about it, the break yet, but it\u2019s coming.\u201d Technically speaking, there is no break for Nurse and his staff. They\u2019ll be coaching Team Giannis this weekend, wiping out any chance for complacency setting in over the All-Star break. \u201c I don\u2019t sense much of that, really, from these guys,\u201d Nurse said. \u201cI think, again, the streak\u2019s keeping it maybe more interesting than maybe it would be, maybe. As for who has the higher ceiling between the two of them, that\u2019s a tough one. They\u2019re both All-Star talents and potential two-way monsters as they continue to mature and add to their respective games. It\u2019s a great dilemma for the Celtics to have. That said, the general consensus among most of the league insiders I consult with is that Tatum has the higher ceiling while Brown is regarded as the most complete and more efficient two-way player. Celtics fans should ignore the debate and enjoy having both of them on the roster for years to come. I guess like most people, I tend to place a higher value on offense than I do defense. I think we\u2019ve been conditioned to value offense over defense throughout the years, even if it wasn\u2019t done on purpose. But the scoring leader always seems to draw more attention than the Kia Defensive Player of the Year. I\u2019m not sure if you can eliminate that subconscious bias that is routinely applied. But I appreciate you raising the question. From:  Joseph Paolino Feb. 4, 2020 | 6:44 p.m. I wanted to share how I have been evaluating the MVP race based solely on numbers. I begin by extracting the statistics of all NBA players this season. Then I remove all players who currently do not qualify for MVP voting (58 out of 82 games (~70.7%) is required, so any player who hasn\u2019t played 70.7% of their team\u2019s games is removed. I also remove any player averaging less than 24 mpg, as I am really only looking to compare the players who are making a significant impact every game they play. After doing this based on the most up to date stats, there were 149 players remaining. I focus only on five per-game statistics: Points, rebounds, assists, steals, and blocks. I sort each of the categories and assign point values from 149 to 1. So, the guy with the highest ppg gets a value of 149, the second-highest gets 148 and so on. I do this for all five categories, then add up the totals and see who\u2019s on top. To factor in winning, I multiply the final player value by the team\u2019s winning percentage (plus 1). For instance, Giannis had a total value of 660 and the Bucks have a winning percentage of 85.7. So, I multiply 660 x 1.857 to get Giannis\u2019 total consideration for MVP. I also am a big believer in the best ability is availability, so I like to factor in games played as well. I take the above value and multiply it by the percentage of games the player has played for his team. (FYI, Kawhi has played in 76% of the Clippers\u2019 games this year, and anybody who doesn\u2019t play in one out of every four games should receive much less consideration than guys who impact 90+% of their team\u2019s games). It\u2019s not perfect and I\u2019m still tweaking the formula to try to make it more accurate, but here is how my ladder looks based on the stats: 1. Giannis Antetokounmpo: 1126 pts 2. LeBron James: 1079 pts 3. James Harden: 1029 pts 4. Nikola Jokic: 1023 pts 5. Bam Adebayo: 1015 pts 6. Anthony Davis: 977 pts 7. Ben Simmons: 961 pts 8. Jimmy Butler: 926 pts 9. Jayson Tatum: 901 pts 10. Russell Westbrook: 879 pts 11. Shai Gilgeous-Alexander: 851 pts 12. Kawhi Leonard: 836 pts 13. Domantas Sabonis: 787 pts 14. Will Barton: 777 pts 15. Pascal Siakam: 775 pts (Luka Doncic was No. 16, Rudy Gobert No. 20, Donovan Mitchell No. 22, Chris Paul No. 23, Damian Lillard No. 24 and Kemba Walker No. 25 by my calculations.) I think the top end is decently accurate and I will keep tweaking it to see if it gets any better. One side note: I am a hardcore Celtics fan, and watch every minute of every game. If you are going to consider any Celtic for MVP, it should be Tatum. He has truly been the best player for them factoring in both ends of the floor and games played. * * * My response:  That\u2019s a lot to digest, Joseph. Thanks for putting in the work because it was a nice empirical wrinkle to add to an already full load of data that we all consume trying to understand the game. It is interesting to see how high guys like Adebayo and Gilgeous-Alexander are on your list. Again, I think there is something to be said for the alternative views (something other than just raw or simple advanced stats). The best part is that there really is no wrong answer. We can all apply whatever elements we\u2019d like to make a case. Perhaps some enterprising genius such as yourself with come up with a foolproof formula for picking the Kia MVP. The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":28818,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/chicago-week-chicago-mecca-basketball","date":"2020-02-10","title":"Why Chicago is the real Mecca of basketball","content":" CHICAGO \u2014 Eddie Johnson was almost done with his run, and he wanted to finish strong. So with legs pumping, head down, the lanky forward from Westinghouse High School pushed hard down a back street in the West Side neighborhood where he spent his teenage years. He sprinted until he got the sense that, hmm, he no longer was alone. \u201cI ran into a gang meeting, it was probably 1,000 guys deep,\u201d Johnson said. \u201cThey looked at me, and some of them were getting ready to get physical.\u201d Yikes. From what he describes, you conjure up Cyrus and the \u201cCan you dig it?!\u201d scene from \u201cThe Warriors.\u201d Only instead of the Baseball Furies or the Rogues, this was real. Maybe the Vice Lords. Or the Four Corner Hustlers, just a couple of the street gangs prominent in the city\u2019s \u201cL-Town\u201d area. Then something strange happened: Nothing.  \u201cAbout 10 guys in the crowd noticed me and knew what I was doing,\u201d Johnson recalled. \u201cThey let me go through. \u2018Don\u2019t bother him,\u2019 guys were telling other guys. They knew I had an opportunity to do something. And they didn\u2019t bother me.\u201d Johnson got a pass that day. Enough of the hard cases in the crowd respected the dream he was chasing \u2014 from Westinghouse to the University of Illinois to the NBA, where he played 17 seasons and scored 19,202 points \u2014 that they cut him slack, same as they have for players before and in the four decades since. It\u2019s a sign of the reverence vast segments of the local population have for the sport, the high regard that bolsters a case made constantly by players and coaches with roots here. More than any other city in the nation, they contend Chicago is the \u201cMecca of basketball.\u201d With NBA All-Star 2020 descending on United Center (home of the Bulls) and Wintrust Arena (where DePaul University\u2019s teams and the WNBA\u2019s Chicago Sky play) this week, a familiar refrain that Chicago might be the center of basketball\u2019s universe packs some extra context and credibility. Not that certain proponents ever are shy about making the claim. \u201cIn Chicago, it means a lot more to us because we are a basketball city and we are the Mecca of basketball, and you can quote me on that,\u201d Lakers big man Anthony Davis said back in July. A product of the Englewood neighborhood on the South Side, Davis was back in town to work with a Nike youth camp for kids ages 8-17. He hadn\u2019t participated in anything like that in his grade school years, coming up the grittier Chicago way, indoors or (most of the time) out. \u201cIt\u2019s basketball in any condition, you know you gonna find a way to play,\u201d Davis told a gathering of reporters. \u201cNo matter if it\u2019s hot, it\u2019s freezing cold in the gym, outside it\u2019s raining, whatever. \u2026 By any means necessary, we wanna play the game.\u201d Clippers coach Doc Rivers, a high school All-American from just outside the city limits in Maywood, Ill., backed Davis\u2019 take entirely. \u201cYeah, he\u2019s right. It\u2019s not even a question,\u201d Rivers said. \u201cThe Mecca of basketball is absolutely Chicago players. New York gets all the rub, which I don\u2019t get, but Chicago, it\u2019s not even close.\u201d The talent pipeline that delivered everyone from George Mikan and Cazzie Russell to Jabari Parker and Davis was pumping particularly well during Rivers\u2019 heyday. \u201cYou play against everybody,\u201d Rivers said. \u201cI mean, how many kids can say, unless you grew up in Chicago, you can have a pickup game out in the park with Isiah Thomas, Terry Cummings, Maurice Cheeks, Mark Aguirre, Darrell Walker. There\u2019s not a lot of pickup games like that in your same grade. That\u2019s every year. That\u2019s called Chicago basketball.\u201d Look, this is highly subjective and largely recreational. Basketball fans across the country can argue for their towns as Meccas and no one is going to break the ties. New York? Of course. Los Angeles is the glamour market of the NBA, the site of so many players\u2019 offseason homes and training regimens. Boston, Philadelphia and Detroit can muster cases too. And if this version of the debate weren\u2019t so NBA-centric, Indiana and North Carolina might try to crash the party as state entries. But this is Chicago\u2019s time to shine. And to brag. \u201cLook at how many players we\u2019ve put in the league,\u201d said former NBA swingman Kendall Gill, raised just south of the city in Olympia Fields. \u201cIt\u2019s like we never stop sending guys to the league.\u201d With each one, the fraternity grows. \u201cDefinitely,\u201d Gill said. \u201cWhen Dwyane Wade was first in the league, I was at the end of my career. But when he saw me, he said \u2018Hey Chi-town.\u2019 And I said, \u2018Hey, what\u2019s up young fella?\u2019 The young players know about the guys before them, and it\u2019s always going to be repeated.\u201d The city has a hoops history that can offer compelling evidence for Mecca status. Consider: The Bulls weren\u2019t an instant hit with paying customers, but by their fourth season, they had assembled the nucleus of a contender that reached the conference semifinals or finals for five consecutive seasons. They won no titles, but the crew of Jerry Sloan, Norm Van Lier, Bob Love, Chet Walker and Tom Boerwinkle played some of the fiercest, most exciting games in franchise history. Until 1984 or thereabouts. \u201cTickets were very easy to come by,\u201d said Johnson, who was 12 when his mother moved the family from the Cabrini Green housing projects closer to Chicago Stadium. \u201cBob Love and Chet Walker were guys that I idolized growing up \u2014 Love was so smooth, and Walker with his 55 pump fakes.\u201d More than a decade earlier, Tony Allen had laid the foundation for his 14-year NBA career at Crane High in the Chicago Public League. He praised the summer leagues in the city for developing talent and whipping up excitement. \u201cMan, just to back up what AD said, we\u2019ve got an infamous Pro-Am league,\u201d Allen told NBA.com. \u201cIt was just some of the best ballplayers you could name that came through and played. Don\u2019t forget about our infamous documentary story, the Arthur Agee story [\u201cHoop Dreams\u201d documentary] \u2026 We were the first ones to put reality TV on the basketball side of things.\u201d Johnson, longtime broadcast analyst for the Phoenix Suns and a co-host on Sirius XM\u2019s NBA channel, also shared memories of summer basketball, dating back to the league at Malcolm X College. Then it was Chicago State, and later, as his pro career unfolded, Illinois Institute of Technology. \u201cI tell you, the gym was packed every night and you had the best of the best playing there,\u201d Johnson said. \u201cEvery NBA player who was from Chicago would come back and play there. It kept us in shape, but it was a tremendous environment because it was packed.\u201d Especially from 1987 to 1989, when Michael Jordan came back to town and played \u2014 and notably in his final appearance. Shelly Stark, described by Johnson as a cornerstone of Chicago\u2019s summer-league offerings, was there the night Jordan locked horns with a young Tim Hardaway. Hardaway, another South Sider and son of Chicago playground \u201clegend\u201d Donald Hardaway, had wrapped up at Texas-El Paso and was headed to Golden State. \u201cBut on the South Side Tim was huge, and, man, you should have been there for that game,\u201d Stark told the Chicago Reader in 2003. \u201cWe had people coming in from all over, lining up outside the door, to see MJ and Hardaway free of charge.\u201d Stark said each player scored 45 points and Jordan\u2019s team won by two. Jordan\u2019s impact in growing basketball\u2019s popularity in Chicago is undeniable. The season before he was drafted, in a Bears and Cubs town, the Bulls drew an average of 6,365 fans. In Jordan\u2019s first two seasons, that bumped to about 11,600 per game, with about 7,000 unsold seats most nights in the cavernous Stadium. But by 1987-88, attendance had nearly tripled in four years to 18,061.  NBA commissioner Adam Silver was studying at the University of Chicago during Jordan\u2019s early years. \u201cPrior to that 1988 NBA All-Star game [at the Stadium],\u201d Silver recently told NBC Sports Chicago, \u201cI attended lots of games with my friends in law school. My recollection is it was not difficult to get tickets. Even for the great Michael Jordan, you could go to the ticket window before the game.\u201d In the 1990s, though, the Bulls broke through, winning six championships in eight seasons with Jordan, Scottie Pippen, Phil Jackson and the rest. Kids throughout the country wanted to \u201c Be Like Mike ,\u201d but nowhere more than in Chicago. \u201cMichael Jordan came up and put those championships in our city, and showed our own youth, man,\u201d said Allen, who was nine when the first 3-peat started and 16 when the second one ended. \u201cI remember running outside and everybody just going crazy. That motivated cats like myself and players all over the world.\u201d Still, Jordan was born in Brooklyn (he turns 57 on Feb. 17). His family soon moved to Wilmington, N.C. He doesn\u2019t qualify for any all-time, All-Chicago team . Neither, for that matter, does Love, Walker, Pippen or Dennis Rodman. Same with Kevin Garnett, who tore it up in his one year at Farragut Academy \u2014 the nation\u2019s No. 1 prospect, leading to a Hall of Fame-worthy NBA career \u2014 but spent his first 17 years in Mauldin, S.C.  No, the best to ever come from and represent Chicago have deeper roots. Like Aguirre, Cummings, Davis, Wade and Rose. And Thomas. \u201cIsiah, he took it to a new level,\u201d Johnson said. \u201cHe was a true prodigy. His ball handling, his persona, all as a young man. I played him 1-on-1 when he was in eighth grade and I was a junior, and he almost beat me. I had to be real physical with him to beat him.\u201d Thomas checked all the boxes of Chicago basketball bona fides. He was one of nine children, raised by his mother Mary on the West Side, not far from the asphalt court at Gladys Park. Money was tight, food was scarce. Mary Thomas became the stuff of legends and made-for-TV movies when she faced down, with a sawed-off shotgun, a group of Vice Lords who had come knocking to recruit her sons. Seeking better for her youngest son, she steered Thomas to the suburb of Westchester \u2014 a 90-minute commute each way by bus and train \u2014 to St. Joseph\u2019s High School. That\u2019s where he starred and earned a scholarship to Indiana, where he took coach Bob Knight and the Hoosiers to the NCAA title as a sophomore in 1981. Now imagine Rose getting drafted by the Pistons instead of the Bulls in 2008. That\u2019s essentially what happened with Thomas in 1981, when Detroit selected him second overall in the Draft, four spots ahead of Chicago. The most successful player in the city\u2019s history \u2014 12-time All-Star, two championships, Hall of Fame \u2014 wound up exiled to a rival, often feeling like a villain in his own town once Jordan moved in. \u201cWe all grew up Bulls fans,\u201d Thomas said several years ago. \u201cBut my mom and my brothers \u2026 sometimes I would look up in the stands and see them clapping for the Bulls, and I\u2019d be like, \u2018What the hell? I gave y\u2019all them tickets!\u2019 \u201cYour friends would tell you after you got them tickets, \u2018I\u2019m coming to the game, but you know we\u2019re gonna beat y\u2019all tonight.\u2019 And I\u2019d say, \u2018I\u2019m part of y\u2019all, right?\u2019 And they\u2019d say, \u2018No, we\u2019re with the Bulls.\u2019 It was nice to go home and just light it up because you knew after the game, if you lost, they were going to give it to you.\u201d Basketball took Thomas out of Chicago but nothing could take Chicago out of him. Some see his smile and on occasion wonder about his sincerity. But no one questions the authenticity of his trek from mean streets to posh boulevards across 50 years in the game. Chicago might be the Mecca, but if you grew up the way Thomas did, getting out was better than getting \u201cnext!\u201d \u201cWhat makes the journey special in Chicago,\u201d Johnson said, \u201cis navigating the games and navigating the peers that would try to get you to do something you didn\u2019t want to do. To get past that and be smart enough to not get caught up in anything.\u201d That applied to the city more than players raised in the less-risky suburbs of Chicagoland. But eventually, all hoops roads led to the same place. \u201cThere was never any prejudice in basketball in Chicago,\u201d Johnson said. \u201cIf you could play, you could play. We judged you on that. And how tough you were. \u201cCould you deal with a really intense situation? We\u2019d go into neighborhoods that weren\u2019t the best, guys would get physical with us, elbows get thrown to where you\u2019re going to have a fight. What do you do? Do you keep playing hard?\u201d In this town you do. Or you\u2019re done. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. As fertile as the area has been in sending talent to the NBA, as rich as the traditions have been in producing players who have become household names, the trick has been differentiating Chicago from \u201cChicagoland,\u201d and then determining who\u2019s in and who\u2019s out. If you stick with the strict boundaries of the Windy City as drawn by the politicians and bureaucrats (always potential trouble here) and go either by place of birth or high school, you cut out heavyweight candidates such as Dwyane Wade, Michael Finley, Doc Rivers and more who developed a few miles or even blocks outside the city\u2019s hardcore hoops scene. But if you open it up too far \u2014 to Illinois products such as Jack Sikma (Kankakee), Jerry Sloan (McLeansboro), Doug Collins (Benton) and Andre Iguodala (Springfield) \u2014 then you\u2019re no longer talking about Chicago. So you wind up defining the eligible area, and the candidates, by what\u2019s considered a manageable daily trek downtown, accepting no one who lived beyond the commuter railroad lines\u2019 last stops. Here then are my first, second and third teams of All-Chicago NBA players, based on growing up in the greater metro area \u2014 and thus, excluding even Bulls superstar Michael Jordan, forever a North Carolina guy. * * * Isiah Thomas  Born : Chicago High school : St. Joseph NBA stats : 19.2 ppg | 3.6 rpg | 9.3 apg, 1981-1994 Thomas arguably is one of the most underrated players in NBA history \u2014 he was an All-Star in 12 of his 13 seasons, with five All-NBA selections and two rings \u2014 but he generally is considered the best player ever from Chicago. Thomas went to high school about 14 miles from the impoverished West Side neighborhood where he grew up but that doesn\u2019t matter. He is fifth all-time in assists average and ranks near the top of unofficial NBA categories for inch-for-inch quality and toughness. Dwyane Wade  Born : Chicago High school : Richards NBA stats : 22.0 ppg | 4.7 rpg | 5.4 apg, 2003-2019 Wade technically grew up outside the city limits \u2014 in Robbins, Illinois \u2014 but nobody is challenging his status as a first-team Chicago elite. He was a 13-time All-Star and three-time champ who\u2019ll head to Springfield, Mass., as soon as he\u2019s eligible. Wade scored more points in the NBA (23,165) than anyone from his hometown and blocked more shots (885) than anyone his size (6-foot-4) or smaller in league history. Mark Aguirre  Born : Chicago High school : Westinghouse NBA stats : 20.0 ppg | 5.0 rpg | 3.1 apg, 1981-1994 \u201cThe Muffin Man\u201d triggered the resurgence of DePaul basketball and is revered by fellow members of the Chicago NBA alumni. Aguirre was a low-post, wide-bottom threat with Dallas, averaging 24.6 points with three All-Star appearances in eight seasons. But when buddy Thomas got the Pistons to acquire him in 1989, Aguirre adjusted his game (12.9 ppg in five seasons) but helped push Detroit over the top to NBA titles that spring and again in 1990. Terry Cummings  Born : Chicago High school : Carver NBA stats : 16.4 ppg | 7.3 rpg | 1.9 apg, 1982-2000 Cummings was a man among boys in college and even when he arrived in the NBA, beating out Hall of Famers James Worthy and Dominique Wilkins as 1983 Rookie of the Year. He was a two-time All-Star who went to the playoffs 13 times in 18 seasons, and he ranks third in points (19,460) and fifth in rebounds (8,630) among Chicago products. George Mikan  Born : Joliet High school : Quigley  NBA stats : 23.1 ppg | 13.4 rpg | 2.8 apg, 1948-1956 Mikan was the NBA\u2019s first superstar, a player whose size (6-foot-10, 245 pounds) put his name on marquees in the league\u2019s early years. He led the Minneapolis Lakers to five titles in seven seasons, and the only reason he didn\u2019t go 6-for-6 in All-Star berths is that the game didn\u2019t exist in Mikan\u2019s first two seasons. He might have won four or five MVPs but that honor wasn\u2019t created until Mikan\u2019s final season. * * * Derrick Rose  Born : Chicago High school : Simeon Career Academy NBA stats : 18.8 ppg | 3.4 rpg | 5.6 apg, 2008-Current Rose\u2019s career was broken for a while, but he remains the only Chicago product to be named NBA Most Valuable Player (2011). That traditionally is a ticket to the Hall of Fame, which Rose \u2014 who missed 318 of 624 games from 2012-2019 \u2014 seemed unlikely to achieve. But he has been a borderline All-Star and Kia Sixth Man candidate for Detroit this season, either of which would polish his resume.  Maurice Cheeks  Born : Chicago High school : Du Sable NBA stats : 11.1 ppg | 2.8 rpg | 6.7 apg, 1978-1993 It took Cheeks 21 years after he retired to reach the Hall of Fame, awfully long for a four-time All-Star, five-time All-Defensive pick and the floor general of Philadelphia\u2019s 1983 championship squad. Cheeks ranks 13th all-time in assists, fifth in steals and 60th in career FG percentage (.5234) \u2014 the top point guard on that list (Magic Johnson ranks 70th).  Michael Finley  Born : Melrose Park High school : Proviso East NBA stats : 15.7 ppg | 4.4 rpg | 2.9 apg, 1995-2010 Finley was definitely a \u201cChicagoland\u201d product, coming from Proviso East in Maywood \u2014 from which a dozen NBA players including Doc Rivers, Jim Brewer, Shannon Brown and Steven Hunter graduated. The two-time All-Star who spent nine seasons with Dallas won his ring in 2007 with San Antonio.  Antoine Walker  Born : Chicago High school : Mount Carmel NBA stats : 17.5 ppg | 7.7 rpg | 3.5 apg, 1996-2008 Walker\u2019s production, while good, never reached its potential, just as his Celtics only reached .500 twice in his first seven seasons. He often wasn\u2019t in great shape and was done at 31, but he earned a late ring with Miami in 2006. Anthony Davis  Born : Chicago High school : Perspectives Charter School NBA stats : 24.0 ppg | 10.4 rpg | 2.2 apg, 2012-Current With Rose as one of two active players here, Davis has done enough to merit this spot. He\u2019s a seven-time All-Star, assumed to be a future MVP, a monster in efficiency ratings and already 59th in blocks (a stat not tracked until 1973-74). Still ringless, though, with one series victory in two trips to the playoffs. * * *  Tim Hardaway  Born : Chicago High school : Carver NBA stats : 17.7 ppg | 3.3 rpg | 8.2 apg, 1989-2003 Only Thomas and Cheeks passed for more assists among the Chicago guys than Hardaway (7,095). A five-time All-NBA choice who missed a full season in his prime to injury (1993-94), he was part of Golden State\u2019s exciting \u201cRun TMC\u201d crew in the 90s with Mitch Richmond and Chris Mullin. Seventeen years after he retired, his 1,542 3-pointers still lead all Chicagoans.  Jeff Hornacek  Born : Elmhurst High school : Lyons Township NBA stats : 14.5 ppg | 3.4 rpg | 4.9 apg, 1986-2000 Notes : Hornacek hails from the second ring of Chicago suburbs and is remembered most as shooting guard on Utah\u2019s Karl Malone-John Stockton teams that reached the Finals in 1997 and 1998. He was an All-Star with Phoenix in 1992. And he happens to be the only alumnus of his massive high school in LaGrange \u2014 to the chagrin of this writer, a fellow alum \u2014 to reach the NBA.  Juwan Howard  Born : Chicago High school : Chicago Vocational Career Academy NBA stats : 13.4 ppg | 6.1 rpg | 2.2 apg, 1994-2013 Nineteen seasons, eight teams and Top 10 spots in scoring, rebounds and minutes among the Chicago players. A one-time All-Star and one-time champion (2012 with Miami), Howard\u2019s greatest fame probably was as one of the \u201cFab Five\u201d freshmen recruits at Michigan in 1991.  Eddie Johnson  Born : Chicago High school : Westinghouse NBA stats : 16.0 ppg | 4.0 rpg | 2.1 apg, 1981-1999 Johnson, who teamed with Aguirre at Westinghouse and followed him into the NBA 28 picks apart in the 1981 Draft, played for six franchises across 17 seasons (with one year in Greece). No rings, never an All-Star, but he was the league\u2019s Sixth Man winner in 1989.  Dan Issel  Born : Batavia High school : Batavia NBA stats : 22.6 ppg | 9.1 rpg | 2.4 apg, 1970-1985 The \u201cyeah but\u201d responses to Issel come down to two things: Batavia is 40 miles west of United Center, far from Chicago\u2019s mean streets. But it is a suburb as \u201cChicagoland\u201d continues its sprawl north, south and west. The other objection is Issel spent six of his 15 pro seasons in the ABA. But he dominated that league, was a workhorse for Denver once the Nuggets entered the NBA in 1976-77 and by far is the points leader (27,482) among Chicago products. If you stripped out his ABA tenure, he\u2019d still rank 14th in points in nine pure NBA seasons on a Chicago list where everybody above him played at least 12. Tony Allen, Nick Anderson, Quinn Buckner, Dave Corzine, Kendall Gill, Rickey Green, Hersey Hawkins, Johnny (Red) Kerr, Corey Maggette, Jahlil Okafor, Kevin Porter, Quentin Richardson, Cazzie Russell, Glenn (Doc) Rivers, Darrell Walker, Patrick Beverley. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. James \u2014 a two-time gold medalist and three-time Olympian \u2014 is among 44 players announced by USA Basketball on Monday as finalists for the team that will play in the Tokyo Games this summer. James played in 2004, 2008 and 2012, helping the U.S. win gold in his most recent two appearances. The pool includes 19 players who have won a total of 31 gold medals in either Olympic or World Cup competition for the Americans \u2014 including nine players who captured the Olympic title for USA Basketball in Rio de Janeiro four years ago. \u201cI\u2019ve always maintained that equity is important,\u201d USA Basketball managing director Jerry Colangelo told The Associated Press . \u201cAnd you earn equity by participating. So, we think they\u2019ve earned the right to be named to the overall roster for USA Basketball. It\u2019s pretty elite. It\u2019s a tremendous pot and the good news is they\u2019ve all said they want to play.\u201d James has played in 68 games for the U.S. national team and has said several times over the past year or so that he is thinking about another Olympics \u2014 after sitting out the Rio Games for a variety of reasons. Part of the interest in participating this summer is to have the chance to play for U.S. coach Gregg Popovich of the San Antonio Spurs. \u201cIt\u2019s a possibility,\u201d James said in December. There will be no tryouts. USA Basketball will pare the list down to a 12-person Olympic team by early June, those decisions to be made by a selection committee \u2014 and to be based in part on player availability and health. Training camp will start in early July, potentially as little as two weeks after the end of the NBA Finals. The Olympics start July 24. USA Basketball officials, including Colangelo and Popovich of the San Antonio Spurs, have met with players in a variety of ways over the past several weeks. Some talks have been directly with players, some with their agents, some with their NBA clubs, sometimes a combination thereof \u2014 and, Colangelo noted, there were some instances where players reached out directly to express interest. If they\u2019re on the list, that means USA Basketball is convinced they want to play. \u201cI feel very good about the response,\u201d Colangelo said. Also included in the pool: 15 of the 16 players from the U.S. who are scheduled to play in Sunday\u2019s NBA All-Star Game, and all 12 members of the team that represented the U.S. at last summer\u2019s Basketball World Cup in China \u2014 where the Americans managed only a seventh-place finish after roughly three dozen players who were on the list at some point dropped out along the way. The Olympics could be a return to the court for players like Durant and Thompson, who have missed the entirety of this NBA season with injuries. Durant\u2019s recovery from Achilles\u2019 surgery is at the point where he\u2019s doing some on-court work and Thompson has said he would like to play if his surgically repaired knee is up to the challenge. Red-hot Raptors aim for 15 straight Earlier this season, the Milwaukee Bucks put the NBA world on notice by ringing up 18 straight wins in roughly a month\u2019s time. These days, the Toronto Raptors are trying to do likewise as they seek their 15th straight win tonight vs. Minnesota ( 7:30 ET, League Pass ). As with any great team, defense has been key for Toronto \u2014 especially during the win streak. Since Jan. 15, the Raptors are No. 1 in steals (10.5 per game), No. 2 in deflections (19.9 per game), No. 5 in charges drawn (12) and No. 5 in total contested shots . Minnesota will have its work cut out for it, then, as it ranks 21st in turnovers and No. 5 in turnover frequency percentage . 2. Can Jazz win another close one? Just when it looked like Utah was slumping, it has gotten its act together. A controversial three-point win on Friday vs. Portland was followed up with a thrilling one-point win Sunday (thanks to Bojan Bogdanovic\u2019s 3-pointer at the horn ). Lest you think those wins are pure luck, consider that the Jazz are 6-4 in games decided by three points or less this season. The Mavs host the Jazz ( 8:30 ET, League Pass ) and while Luka Doncic remains out, Kristaps Porzingis has been hot during Doncic\u2019s absence (he\u2019s averaging 29 ppg, 11.3 rpg and 1.8 spg since Jan. 31). Porzingis struggled in Dallas\u2019 last game vs. Utah (which, of course, was another close win for the Jazz ) and will no doubt be looking to both bounce back from that showing and keep his hot streak rolling. 3. Bucks dominating sub-.500 teams Milwaukee is setting the standard this season when it comes to a winning team playing one that is its opposite. To date, Milwaukee is an impressive 33-1 against teams under .500 while also taking care of business against winning teams (going 12-6 in those games). The Kings visit the Bucks next ( 8 ET, NBA TV ) and will to not get steamrolled by Milwaukee\u2019s machine-like play. It won\u2019t be easy though \u2026 the Bucks are fresh off their 13th victory of the season without trailing and their 30th double-digit win. They\u2019re also outscoring opponents by 12.5 ppg, which is on pace for the highest point differential in NBA history. 4. Rodeo road trip woes for Spurs In the days leading up to San Antonio\u2019s annual road journey, the team was fresh off solid wins against the Jazz and Hornets. Since then, though, it has been mostly a disaster. The Spurs are 0-4 on the trek, losing two of those game by 20 points or more. A visit to Denver ( 9 ET, League Pass ) would not seem to help things as the Nuggets are 8-3 in their last 11 games. Aside from that, All-Star center Nikola Jokic is rolling : he\u2019s averaging 24.8 ppg, 10.9 rpg and 7.0 apg on 53.7% shooting since Jan. 2. 5. Suns\u2019 Booker in a slump When the All-Star reserves were announced on Jan. 30 , Suns guard Devin Booker was somewhat vocal about being excluded from that group. It\u2019s hard to say exactly how being left off has affected Booker, but the numbers show a dip in scoring production. From the start of the season until Jan. 30, Booker was averaging 27.1 ppg on 51% shooting (36.8% on 3-pointers), 4.1 rpg and 6.4 apg and the Suns were 20-27. Since Jan. 30 , Booker is at 24.3 ppg (45.4% overall; 36.1% on 3-pointers), 5.0 rpg and 5.7 apg and the Suns are 1-5. Maybe tonight\u2019s matchup against the Lakers ( 10:30 ET, NBA TV ) and their All-Star duo of LeBron James and Anthony Davis will get Booker refocused. 6. On this date: Pierce immortalized Every generation of Celtics fans seems to have its iconic player and for those fans from the 2000s, that player was Paul Pierce. On Feb. 10, 2018, Boston officially retired Pierce\u2019s No. 34 jersey after the Cavaliers-Celtics game at TD Garden. Pierce became the 21st Celtics player to have his jersey retired, joining legends such as Larry Bird, Robert Parish, Kevin McHale, Bill Russell, John Havlicek and others."},{"id":28785,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/horry-scale-bojan-bogdanovic-beats-rockets","date":"2020-02-10","title":"Horry Scale: Bojan Bogdanovic's deep 3-pointer stuns Rockets","content":" A reminder on The Horry Scale: It breaks down a game-winning buzzer-beater (GWBB) in the categories of difficulty, game situation \u2014 was the team tied or behind at the time? \u2014 along with importance \u2014 playoff game or garden-variety night in November? \u2014 and celebration. Then we give it an overall grade on a scale of 1-5 Robert Horrys, named for the patron saint of last-second answered prayers. In a matchup of two teams jockeying for position in the middle of the Western Conference, the Jazz and Rockets traded punches down the stretch in Houston, but it was Bojan Bogdanovic who delivered the wild knockout blow. After P.J. Tucker hit a corner 3-pointer with 1.6 seconds left, it appeared the Rockets had salvaged the win after fighting back down the stretch. But Jazz coach Quin Snyder drew up a perfect out-of-bounds play, and Bogdanovic came off the curl to hit a miraculous 29-footer at the buzzer to give Utah the 114-113 win at the Toyota Center. Through three quarters, though, Bogdanovic would have seemed like an unlikely hero. Bogdanovic, who averages 21.2 points, was struggling through one of the worst scoring games of the season, scoring two points through the first three quarters. Bogdanovic saved his best for last, though, and finished with eight, none bigger than the 3 that earned him his entry on the Horry Scale. \u201cBogie has been big for us all year,\u201d Mike Conley told NBA TV afterward. \u201cEveryone had the confidence in him to get the ball to him. He\u2019s a big-time player and that\u2019s just a fun win to be a part of.\u201d DIFFICULTY: The game-winner couldn\u2019t have been more difficult. Not only did he have two defenders draped all over him, but Bogdanovic may have even been fouled on the play. After setting a pindown screen for Donovan Mitchell (whom the Rockets defended well), Bogdanovic raced to the top of the 3-point circle, caught the inbound pass from Joe Ingles and barely had time to set his feet before launching an off-balanced shot over the outstretched arm of James Harden. The shot was dead on, and didn\u2019t touch rim as it silenced the crowd in Houston. \u201dThe play was designed for me to shoot a 3,\u201d Bogdanovic said. \u201dIt\u2019s big-time from their part to have that trust and confidence in me to make that shot after the game that I had.\u201d GAME SITUATION: The Rockets and Jazz had exchanged punches down the stretch of a back-and-forth thriller. Robert Covington had given Houston the lead with 28 seconds left, but moments later Donovan Mitchell hit a pair of free throws to give Utah a 111-110 lead. The Rockets seemed set to end a two-game losing streak when P.J. Tucker hit a clutch 3-pointer with 1.6 seconds left. But the Rockets left too much time on the clock, and Utah\u2019s coaching staff believed in Bogdanovic, despite his struggles. \u201cI wasn\u2019t supposed to be on the court, the way I played all game,\u201d Bogdanovic said in his on-court interview. \u201cBut the coaching staff believed in me. I stayed confident. I know I\u2019m able to hit those shots.\u201d CELEBRATION: This isn\u2019t Bogdanovic\u2019s first buzzer-beater this season, but the celebration still seemed appropriate and spontaneous. After the bucket dropped, Bogdanovic turned and stared toward the other end of the floor as his Jazz teammates raced toward him. Bogdanovic was able to avoid their initial onrush, untucked his jersey and jumped up and down before being mobbed near the scorer\u2019s table. The best part of the celebration, though, may have been the reaction from the other team, as P.J. Tucker slumped to the ground, appearing to cover his mouth in awe as if to chuckle in amazement at the dagger. \u201dI knew it was good as soon as he caught it,\u201d Tucker said. \u201dHe got a good lift on it. I could tell as soon as he shot it.\u201d GRADE: This Horry Scale entry had it all: Two Western Conference teams trying to figure things out, fighting for playoff positioning. A beautifully drawn up play, designed for a player who struggled the entire game. Near-perfect defense with two players guarding the shooter as tightly as possible. A perfect splash, followed by an emotional celebration by the road team. When asked after the game how Bogdanovic would rate his game-winner, the sixth-year forward said, \u201cBetter than the Milwaukee one.\u201d Well, who are we to argue with Bogie? We graded that night\u2019s corner 3-pointer with \u201c4 Horrys\u201d \u2026 so this one gets a bump. 4.5 Horrys . To fortify their front line, the Hornets have recalled forwards Caleb Martin and Jalen McDaniels from the Greensboro Swarm."},{"id":28814,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/no-engine-failure-kobe-bryant-crash","date":"2020-02-08","title":"Probe: Kobe Bryant helicopter was 100 feet from clear skies","content":"LOS ANGELES ( AP ) \u2014 A witness to the deadly crash of a helicopter carrying Kobe Bryant and eight others said it sounded normal just before slamming into a hillside and wreckage examined by experts at the scene showed no sign of engine failure, federal investigators said in a report released Friday. The Jan. 26 crash occurred in cloudy conditions and aviation experts said the \u201cinvestigative update\u201d from the National Transportation Safety Board reinforces the notion the pilot became disoriented and crashed while trying to get to clear skies around Calabasas, northwest of Los Angeles. The veteran pilot, Ara Zobayan, came agonizingly close to finding his way out of the clouds. He told air traffic control he was climbing to 4,000 feet (1,219 meters). He ascended to 2,300 feet (701 meters), just 100 feet (30 meters) from what camera footage later reviewed by the NTSB showed was the top of the clouds. But rather than continuing higher Zobayan began a high-speed descent and left turn in rapidly rising terrain. He slammed into the hillside at more than 180 mph (290 kph) and was descending at 4,000 feet (1,219 meters) per minute. \u201c\u201cIf you exit the bottom of the clouds at 4000 feet per minute at that high speed, you\u2019ve certainly lost control of the aircraft,\u201d air safety consultant Kipp Lau said. He said Bryant\u2019s chopper could have emerged from the clouds in just 12 more seconds, assuming it was ascending at 500 feet (152.40 meters) per minute. \u201cOnce you break out of the clouds it\u2019s clear. Everything lines up with the body,\u201d Lau said. \u201cNow you have a real horizon.\u201d Mike Sagely, a helicopter pilot in the Los Angeles area with 35 years of flying experience, said the aircraft\u2019s last moments suggest Zobayan had started to execute a maneuver designed to pop above the clouds by flying up and forward. \u201cWhen he went into the clouds, he had a full on emergency,\u201d Sagely said. When pilots try to turn instead of sticking with the pop-up maneuver, \u201cprobably in the neighborhood of 80 to 90% of the time, it\u2019s catastrophic,\u201d he said. The crash occurred as the group was flying to a girls basketball tournament at Bryant\u2019s Mamba Sports Academy. He coached his 13-year-old daughter Gianna\u2019s team. She and two teammates were among the nine people killed. The deaths shook Los Angeles and the sporting world. Within hours, thousands had gathered outside Staples Center, where Bryant starred for the Lakers, and began a makeshift memorial that became a massive display of flowers, candles, personal notes, basketballs and other mementos. A public memorial is scheduled for Feb. 24 at Staples. The date 2\/24 corresponds with the No. 24 jersey he wore and the No. 2 worn by Gianna. The NTSB\u2019s report was a compilation of information and data about the flight, helicopter and pilot. It\u2019s likely to take a year for the NTSB to issue a report about the cause. Zobayan was a regular pilot for Bryant and the chief pilot for Island Express Helicopters, with more than 8,200 hours of flight time. He was certified to fly solely using instruments \u2013 a more difficult rating to attain that allows pilots to fly at night and through clouds when the ground isn\u2019t visible \u2013 and was a pilot to other celebrities including LA Clippers star Kawhi Leonard and Kylie Jenner. During the flight with Bryant\u2019s group, Zobayan did not report any equipment problems and sounded calm while communicating to air traffic controllers. His final transmission said he was going to climb above the clouds. Eight seconds after reaching peak altitude, he started the fateful descent. An unidentified witness told the NTSB the hillside where the crash occurred was shrouded in mist when he heard the helicopter approaching. It sounded normal and he then saw the blue-and-white aircraft emerge from the fog moving forward and down. Within 2 seconds it slammed into the hillside just below him. Former Island Express pilot Kurt Deetz, who regularly flew Bryant to games at the Staples Center, said reading the NTSB\u2019s report reinforced how dangerously fast the helicopter was traveling in conditions that had prompted the Los Angeles Police Department and the county sheriff to ground their copters. \u201cNormally in those conditions, you\u2019re pretty cautious. You\u2019re proceeding slowly,\u201d Deetz said. The impact tore the helicopter apart and all aboard died from blunt force trauma. The aircraft\u2019s instrument panel was destroyed and most of the devices were displaced. The flight controls were broken and suffered fire damage. Investigators believe that since a tree branch at the crash site was cut, the engines were working and rotors turning at the time of impact. All four of the helicopter\u2019s blades had similar damage, the report stated. The 50-year-old Zobayan\u2019s most recent flight review included training on inadvertently flying into bad weather conditions. It covered how to recover if the aircraft\u2019s nose is pointed too far up or down, and what to do if the helicopter banks severely to one side. He earned satisfactory grades in the review, which took place in May 2019. Deetz said Zobayan previously had told him that he did not have actual experience flying in clouds, despite being certified. Deetz said that isn\u2019t uncommon. Bryant\u2019s helicopter did not have a device called the Terrain Awareness and Warning System, known as TAWS, that signals when an aircraft is in danger of hitting ground. The NTSB has recommended the system be mandatory for helicopters but the Federal Aviation Administration only requires it for air ambulances. U.S. Sen. Dianne Feinstein and Rep. Brad Sherman, both California Democrats, have called for the FAA to mandate the devices. While NTSB Member Jennifer Homendy has said it\u2019s not clear if the warning system would have averted the crash, aviation expert Gary Robb said Friday\u2019s report highlights the need to equip all helicopters with the warning system. \u201cIf this helicopter had had TAWS, Mr. Bryant and the rest of the passengers would be alive today,\u201d said Robb, a lawyer who has written a textbook about helicopter crash litigation. The others killed included Orange Coast College baseball coach John Altobelli, his wife, Keri and daughter Alyssa; Bryant\u2019s friend and assistant coach, Christina Mauser; and Sarah Chester and her daughter Payton, 14. A public memorial for the Altobelli family will be held Monday at Angel Stadium in Anaheim."},{"id":28780,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/kobe-bryant-memorial-set-february-24-report","date":"2020-02-08","title":"Kobe Bryant memorial set for Feb. 24 at Staples Center","content":"A public memorial service for Kobe Bryant and his daughter Gianna is planned for Feb. 24 at Staples Center. The Timberwolves will also receive Jacob Evans and Omari Spellman, the teams announced on Thursday. Russell, whom Golden State acquired via sign-and-trade with Brooklyn last offseason, is averaging a career-high 23.6 points along with 6.2 assists and 3.7 rebounds. This marks Russell\u2019s fourth team since being drafted second overall in 2015. An All-Star last season with the Nets, Russell now joins good friend Karl-Anthony Towns on the Timberwolves, giving the franchise a young guard-center duo locked into long-term contracts. Wiggins, meanwhile, will look for a fresh start in San Francisco after failing to live up to the hype of being drafted first overall in 2014. The 24-year-old forward is in the second year of a five-year max contract, averaging 22.4 ppg, 5.2 rpg and 3.7 apg. Golden State has been decimated by injuries to Stephen Curry and Klay Thompson. The Warriors sit in last place in the Western Conference \u2014 just four games behind the Wolves \u2014 but the prospect of their stars\u2019 respective returns and a potential top-four pick this summer could make for a quick turnaround. The Timberwolves, who have lost 12 straight games, have bottomed out in their first year under president of basketball operations Gersson Rosas as he attempts to remake the roster for a faster-paced, 3-point-heavy offense as directed by rookie coach Ryan Saunders. * * * From NBA.com\u2019s Michael C. Wright Main Takeaway: There\u2019s always the question of which team won a trade once it\u2019s consummated, and this transaction could prove to be a win-win for both parties. Impact on Warriors: There was always the question of how Russell would fit with Curry, because the former just isn\u2019t tailor-made for Golden State\u2019s style of play. While Russell is averaging a career-high 23.6 points per game, the Warriors needed a more natural fit, plus they\u2019re also trying to restock the cupboard for more runs at a championship; a goal aided somewhat by the picks they\u2019ll receive. By sending off the Evans and Spellman contracts, Golden State also puts itself in position to avoid the luxury tax, which also would take them out of the repeater tax next season. The top overall pick in 2014, Wiggins has largely been disappointing over his first six seasons. But he\u2019s just 24 years old, and Golden State has the developmental personnel, coaching staff and veteran leadership in the locker room to resurrect Wiggins\u2019 career as a potential starting wing for a revamped Warriors roster. Impact on Timberwolves: The free-falling Timberwolves, with two double-digit losing streaks since the start of December \u2014 needed a young, dynamic threat to pair alongside Towns, and that\u2019s exactly what they receive in this deal for Russell, who is shooting 43% from the floor and 37.4% from deep, in addition to dishing 6.2 assists per game. The Timberwolves had long coveted Russell, and simultaneously were able to shed the contract of Wiggins, who never quite lived up to his promise. Besides that, Russell and Towns are good friends, which should help the pair mesh quickly. Towns had been pretty vocal lately about his disappointment in how Minnesota\u2019s season is going. But if you remember, Minnesota pursued Russell in free agency with Towns heading up the recruitment, before the Timberwolves ultimately lost him to the Warriors. Unfortunately, neither he nor his All-Star teammate, Jimmy Butler, will suit up tonight in Sacramento ( 10 ET, League Pass ) as the Kings get their first look at their new additions ( Jabari Parker and Alex Len ), too. The Heat are expected to be at full strength Sunday against the Trail Blazers, but for tonight, All-Star Bam Adebayo and Co. will do a lot of the heavy lifting against the Kings. 3. A change in the middle for Detroit For the first time since Oct. 31, 2012, the Pistons will not have center Andre Drummond on the court. He was dealt to the Cleveland Cavaliers yesterday as Detroit is fully starting a reset of itself for a new era. The initial look at what that might be comes tonight in Oklahoma City ( 8 ET, League Pass ) as youngsters like Sekou Doumbouya, Bruce Brown, Svi Mykhailiuk, Luke Kennard, Christian Wood and others will get more playing time in various roles. While Detroit is just 4 1\/2 games out of No. 8 in the East as of this writing, the rest of this season is about next season now. 4. New bigs land in Atlanta Clint Capela, welcome aboard . Dewayne Dedmon, welcome back . The Celtics will be the first foe to get a glimpse of the Hawks\u2019 revamped center rotation ( 7:30 ET, League Pass ) and it should be interesting to see how All-Star guard Trae Young benefits from it. Departed center Alex Len received 8.5% of Young\u2019s passes and took 73.9% of his shots from less than 10 feet. In Houston, Capela received passes from Russell Westbrook 13.4% of the time and from James Harden 13.5% of the time . In either case, Capela finished his shots within 10 feet of the rim around 99.2% of the time . All that to say: watch for lots and lots of alley-oops and rim runs from Capela. 5. Will Sixers ever get on track? Philadelphia was fairly quiet at the trade deadline, adding two guards to its mix ( Alec Burks and Glenn Robinson III ) while parting with a rotation player ( James Ennis ). The new faces didn\u2019t play in last night\u2019s blowout loss to the Bucks , which marked the Sixers fourth straight loss. \u201cInside The NBA\u201d analyst Charles Barkley called the Sixers \u201c the Cleveland Browns of the NBA \u201d after their loss and, it\u2019s hard to figure out exactly what\u2019s wrong in Philly . The good news for Philadelphia is it is back home tonight. The bad news is it is playing the surging Memphis Grizzlies ( 7 ET, League Pass ), who seem to be invigorated by their part in the Iguodala trade . 6. Skidding Jazz try to right ship Few teams are more of a current representation of how quickly things can change in the NBA than Utah. On Jan. 25, they scored a thrilling home win against the Dallas Mavericks, giving the Jazz 14 wins in their last 15 games. Since then? They\u2019ve lost five in a row (although only one of those was a blowout). Utah hosts the Blazers next ( 10:30 ET, ESPN ) as part of a four-game, pre-All-Star break stretch that includes the Rockets (Feb. 9), Mavs (Feb. 10) and Heat (Feb. 12).
